Yan et al.240 reported an onion-like carbon anode material from the facile combustion soot as a candle burnt. When used as an anode, the electrode exhibits a high reversible capacity (245 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1). The electrode also showed good rate performance (78 mA h g−1 at 10 A g−1) and cycling stability (111 mA h g−1 at 2 A g−1 over 1000 cycles). After further fabricating into a K+ ion hybrid capacitor using onion-like carbon as a battery-type anode and activated carbon as a capacitor-type cathode, the device could exhibit a high energy density of 142 W h kg−1 with long cycling stability of 83% capacity retention after 6000 cycles.
Nanjundan et al.241 reported an amorphous hard carbon material for high-performance PIBs, prepared via a simple pyrolysis method from commercial cellulose. The high capacity (−294 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1), good rate capability, and long cycling performance (−60 mA h g−1 after 1000 cycles at 1 A g−1) were attributed to the low volume expansion and the enlarged interlayer spacing alongside the electronegative oxygen functional groups on the carbon material. With an intrinsic fibrous structure and nitrogen functional groups, chitin was also used to produce hard carbon anodes for PIBs. Guo et al.242 prepared N-doped carbon nanofibers by direct carbonization of biowaste chitin. The resulting materials possessed a specific capacity of 200 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at a current density of 55.8 mA g−1. Zhang et al.243 prepared a hierarchically porous N-doped carbon microsphere (NCS) using seafood waste as the raw material. The porous microstructure of the NCS was found to promote electron/ion transport, which, coupled with the abundant defects, enhanced K ions’ adsorption capability. Based on the surface-driven K storage mechanism, the NCS anode displayed long cycling stability (180 mA h g−1 at 1.8C) without obvious capacity decay over 4000 cycles and an outstanding rate capability of 154 mA h g−1 at 72C. More recently, porous hard carbon nanobelts with numerous active sites were prepared by Zhang et al. via a self-template assisted pyrolysis strategy from mineralized shrimp shells (Fig. 11a and b).244 The hard carbon nanobelts possess several advantages for the PIB anode, such as pyrrolic/pyridinic-N and O dual-doping, large interlayer spacing, and a hierarchical micro/meso/macro-porous structure. As a result, the reversible capacity recorded for the electrode was 468 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1. The electrode also enjoyed a long cycling life at the high current density of 1 A g−1 (277 mA h g−1 over 1600 cycles) as illustrated in Fig. 11c. As in most biomass-derived carbon, the K+ storage mechanism was proved by DFT calculations to be dominated by capacitive-adsorption processes. The capacitive-adsorption mechanism was enhanced by the rich active sites in the N and O co-doped hard carbon nanobelts. After fabricating into a coin-type potassium dual-ion battery with a commercial expanded graphite cathode, the cell exhibited a stable charge/discharge capacity of 89/84 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1 (Fig. 11d–f).
 Fig. 11  (a) Schematic illustration of the preparation of N/O co-doped porous hard carbon nanobelts (NOCNBs); (b) the adsorption of K+ on the NOCNB anode; (c) long cycling stability of the NOCNB electrodes in PIBs; and (d) the schematic diagram, (e) rate capability, and (f) cycling performance of the NOCNB//expanded graphite potassium dual-ion battery. 
Porous MOFs have also been used as precursors to prepare carbon-based materials via a simple calcination process. However, the different MOF compositions and the various synthesis conditions lead to MOF-derived carbon materials with different morphologies and compositions, further affecting the electrochemical performances. Zeolitic imidazolate frameworks (ZIFs) are probably the most investigated raw materials for MOF-derived carbon for energy storage applications.245 Hu et al.246 reported a hollow carbon nanobubble material with a diameter of 70 nm and an average thickness of 10 nm, prepared by pyrolyzing a Zn-containing MOF (ZIF-8). The porous structure shortened the diffusion pathway and increased the active surface for K+ ions, resulting in a good specific capacity of 100 mA h g−1 at a high current density of 2 A g−1. Cobalt-containing ZIF materials (ZIF-67) were also used to produce N-doped carbon anodes for PIBs by Xu et al.247 Unlike the amorphous ZIF-8 derived carbon nanobubbles, ZIF-67 tends to convert to crystalline graphitic carbon nanotubes because of the catalysis effects of Co. Although the initial specific capacity was similar to that of ZIF-8-derived carbon (102 mA h g−1 at 2 A g−1), the ZIF-67 derived carbon nanotube anode has greater cyclic stability (100 mA h g−1 after 500 cycles). Yang et al.248 also fabricated a high pyridinic N-doped porous carbon anode derived from ZIF-67, which showed excellent electrochemical K storage performances. The electrode could deliver a specific capacity of 587.6 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1, long cycling performance (231.6 mA h g−1 after 2000 cycles at 500 mA g−1), and stable rate capability (186.2 mA h g−1 at 2 A g−1). Sun et al.249 combined the advantages of both ZIF-8 and ZIF-67 derived carbon by designing a novel composite architecture. The new composite, defined as ZIF-8@ZIF-67, has N-doped porous carbon confined within CoP nanoparticles (NC@CoP/NC) and was produced by complex nucleation, carbonization and phosphorization processes successively. The NC@CoP/NC anode exhibited an improved capacity of 200 mA h g−1 at 2 A g−1 and capacity retention of 93% after 100 cycles at 100 mA g−1. Other MOFs, e.g. NH2-MIL-101(Al),250 and Bi-MOF,251 were also used to produce various hard carbons for PIB anodes. Liu et al.251 developed an ultrathin carbon film@carbon nanorod@Bi nanoparticle (UCF@CN@BiN) composite by directly annealing the Bi-MOF at 700 °C in Ar. The carbon nanorods could provide high-speed ion transport channels and accommodate the volumetric change of Bi nanoparticles during the Bi  KBi2  K3Bi2  K3Bi potassiation/depotassiation processes. The reversible capacity of UCF@CN@BiN was recorded to be 425 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1 with a discharge capacity of 327 mA h g−1 over 600 cycles. Due to the abundant variety of MOF materials, future studies should focus on designing novel hard carbon structures to improve the specific capacity and initial Coulombic efficiency.
In order to understand the relationship between the structural characteristics of porous carbon (e.g. pore structure and interlayer distance) and electrochemical performances, Lin et al.252 designed a series of porous carbon materials with various pore structures via a solvothermal method, using 1-dodecanol as the carbon source. The authors found that the intercalation capacity and the adsorption capacity of K+ ions are positively correlated with the IG/ID ratio and mesopore volume, respectively. The interlayer distance, surface area, and pore size play comparatively far less important roles in the K ion storage. Moreover, the mesopores can also promote K ion migration, serve as active sites for the adsorption of K ions, and accommodate the volume expansion during the potassiation/depotassiation process. The electrode fabricated using the materials with the largest mesopore volume and appropriate surface area could display a high capacity (633 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1) and long cycling stability (263.9 mA h g−1 at 1 A g−1 after 1000 cycles). These results might provide a new avenue to design novel carbonaceous materials as high-performance anodes for practical PIBs.
Although less studied than other types of carbon materials, soft carbon has unique advantages for PIB application, such as highly tunable crystallinity and lattice interlayer spacing, exhibiting structural integrity for high energy density and stability in the battery field. Cao et al.253 reported a pitch-derived soft carbon sample by carbonizing pitch powder at 1200 °C in Ar, producing carbon in the form of big aggregates of micro-sized particles. The TEM, XRD, and Raman results indicated that the synthesis protocol successfully increased the carbon crystallinity and prevented lattice large-range ordering. The produced carbon has turbostratic lattices with large interlayer spacing, which is favourable for K+ storage. Unlike other soft carbons where the storage mechanism is dominated by surface adsorption, the induced short-order crystallinity increased the intercalation reactions’ contribution. The intercalation of K+ ions was proved to occur at a low voltage below 1 V, which could ensure a high battery voltage and high energy density in the full battery compared to other non-graphitic carbons. Also, in situ XRD indicated that the formation of intercalation compounds requiring long range-ordered carbon (e.g. KC24) is suppressed, and therefore soft carbon can provide more resistance to structural damage than graphite. The anode fabricated from pitch-derived soft carbon could provide a reversible specific capacity of 296 mA h g−1 at 28 mA g−1, which is higher than that of graphite (273.6 mA h g−1) and hard carbon (148.2 mA h g−1). After 50 cycles, 93.2% of the initial capacity could still be provided by the soft carbon anode, indicating better stability than graphite (only kept 82.6% after 50 cycles). A long-range cycling test over 1000 cycles also showed that soft carbon outperforms both graphite and hard carbon. The rate capability performance was also proved to be better for soft carbon, with the electrode delivering 115.2 mA h g−1 when the current density increases to 1.4 A g−1. Qiu et al.254 prepared a high-performance soft carbon from low-cost coal via a ball milling, acid washing, oxidation, and carbonization process. The as-prepared coal-based carbon anode could deliver a reversible capacity of 260 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1 and a stable long cycling performance with a capacity of 118 mA h g−1 after 1200 cycles at 1 A g−1. Ji and coworkers255 reported the electrochemical performance of a hard–soft carbon microsphere composite with 20 wt% of soft carbon distributed in the hard carbon. The microspheres were produced using sodium carboxymethyl cellulose as a binder. The experimental results indicated that the hard–soft carbon anode could provide better performances at high current than hard carbon. The hard–soft composite carbon provided a capacity of 190 mA h g−1 at 560 mA g−1, which is higher than that of hard carbon (135 mA h g−1). After 200 cycles at 280 mA g−1, the composite carbon anode could maintain a specific capacity close to 200 mA h g−1 with a retention rate of 93%, which is more stable than the pure hard carbon and soft carbon materials.
Another type of soft carbon tested recently for PIB anodes is carbon nanotubes (CNTs).256–259 The volume change of the K ion intercalation in CNTs is about 61%, suggesting that electrodes fabricated from CNTs are subjected to severe mechanical damage and thereby fast capacity fading. To address these challenges, Han et al.260 designed a hierarchical structure based on multi-walled carbon nanotubes by regulating the synthetic parameters of the CVD process. The nanotubes’ inner layer consists of dense graphitic carbon, while the outer layer is composed of disordered amorphous carbon, which is beneficial for hosting K+ ions. The nanotubes were further interconnected into a hyper porous sponge with a high macropore volume, facilitating both the potassiation/depotassiation reaction kinetics and surface capacitive behavior. The hierarchical multi-walled CNTs exhibited a high reversible specific capacity (232 mA h g−1), excellent rate capability, and long cycling stability of at least 500 cycles. Cao and coworkers261 further reported a porous-carbon aerogel constructed from 1D porous CNTs with a pore size distribution of around 0.8 nm. The long cycling stability and high rate capability with enhanced capacity retention were attributed to the high surface capacitive mechanism. The sub-nanopores would also decrease the K ion diffusion and transport energy barrier with a limited lattice change, providing a novel strategy for developing highly efficient carbon-based anodes for energy storage systems.
Heteroatom doping is also a common strategy to modify the K-storage capacity and cycling stability of non-graphite carbon materials. Chen et al.262 prepared N/O co-doped mesoporous amorphous carbon octahedra via a carbonization–etching process, using a metal–organic framework (Cu-BTC) raw material. The resulting materials possessed a hierarchical mesoporous structure, large specific surface area (1411 m2 g−1), and enlarged interlayer spacing (≈0.387 nm). When the octahedra were used as an anode, they could deliver a superior reversible capacity (364 mA h g−1 over 50 cycles at 50 mA g−1), and excellent cyclability (80 mA h g−1 after 3000 cycles at 2 A g−1). Nitrogen and oxygen dual-doped amorphous carbon network materials were also synthesized by Zheng et al.,263 this time by an in situ vapor phase polymerization self-assembly process followed by pyrolysis strategy. The electrode delivered a capacity of 464.9 mA h g−1 at 0.05 A g−1, with excellent rate performance revealed by a capacity of 175 mA h g−1 at a high current density of 5.0 A g−1. The N/O doped amorphous carbon also showed long cycling performance with a capacity decay of less than 0.008% per cycle. The authors determined that pseudocapacitive processes dominate the K-ion storage mechanism. Guo et al.264 were able to control the content and type of doped N in a honeycomb-like carbon structure co-doped with phosphorus. The sufficient pore defects and edges, originating from the porous structure and P-doping, could promote the formation of high-level pyridinic N in carbon, thereby endorsing the interfacial adsorption reaction of K+ ions. An outstanding reversible capacity (419.3 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1) and excellent rate capability (270.4 mA h g−1 at 1 A g−1) could be achieved by the pyridinic N and P doped carbon electrode. Lei et al.265 reported a highly nitrogen-doped carbon nanofiber electrode material by carbonizing N-containing polypyrrole nanofibers at high temperature. After carbonization at 650 °C, the resulting sample (NCNF-650) has a nitrogen content of 13.8%. Increasing the treatment temperature above 650 °C produced materials with lower pyridinic N and pyrrolic N content while increasing the quaternary N. The authors also concluded from DFT calculations that pyridinic N and pyrrolic N would promote the generation of defect sites and contribute to a higher K ion storage capacity than the quaternary N sites. Therefore, the N-doped carbon nanofibers prepared at 650 °C showed the highest reversible specific capacity of 248 mA h g−1 at 25 mA g−1 and rate performance (101 mA h g−1 at 20 A g−1) when compared with nanofibers prepared at higher temperatures. After 4000 discharge/charge cycles at 2 A g−1, the NCNF-650 anode could keep a capacity of 146 mA h g−1 with a Coulombic efficiency of almost 100%. Alshareef et al.266 reported a molecular scale site-selective doping strategy to produce a defect rich and edge N doped carbon anode via a copolymerization pyrolysis procedure. The N atom doping could reach more than 10 at% in carbon with an edge-N ratio of 87.6%. The edge and defect-rich N-doped carbon anode delivered an excellent reversible potassium storage capacity (423 mA h g−1), long stable cycle life (93.8% retention after three months) and superior rate capability. Kaskel et al.267 also proposed edge enriched N-doped porous carbon nanosheets based on a pyridine-coordinated polymer pyrolysis-etching strategy. The optimized amorphous carbon with a high surface area has a nitrogen content on the edge of 9.34 at%. The electrode showed an ultra-long cyclic life with a capacity retention of 94.5% after 6000 cycles at a current density of 1 A g−1. All these studies could offer a potential approach to adjusting the N active atom content in heteroatom-doped carbon materials for PIB energy storage systems.
In addition to doping carbon with N and O, S-doped and P-doped non-graphitic carbon materials have also been investigated as an anode for PIBs. Deng et al.268 prepared a high sulfur-doped hard carbon with sulfur content as high as 25.8 wt% by heating glucose in a K2SO4–LiCl-KCl molten salt. The S-doped hard carbon anode exhibited a specific capacity of 361.4 mA h g−1 in the first cycle and retained 88% of the capacity after the 100th cycle at 50 mA g−1. Nan et al.269 reported S-doped carbon by heating dry bamboo and sulfur powder under Ar. Both S and residual O atoms on the bamboo charcoal surface provided active sites that facilitated the interaction between K+ and the anode. To improve the low-voltage plateau capacity, P-doped hard carbon was employed as the anode material for PIBs.270 The P-doped carbon was prepared by stirring lignin-derived hard carbon with H3PO4 followed by sintering at 1300 °C. The low-voltage capacity below 0.25 V improved from 153 to 192 mA h g−1 after doping. The specific capacity also increased from 245 to 302 mA h g−1 after doping. Our group recently reported a carbonization–etching strategy for making a class of S and O co-doped porous hard carbon microsphere (PCM) anodes.271 The amorphous PCMs possessed a porous architecture with a large BET surface area of 983.2 m2 g−1, an enlarged interlayer distance of 0.393 nm, and abundant structural defects. These features contributed to excellent K storage, including a high capacity of 226.6 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1 over 100 cycles and a highly stable extended cycling capacity of 108.4 mA h g−1 after 2000 cycles at 1 A g−1. The reversible capacities at 50, 200, 500, and 1000 mA g−1 were 230, 213, 176, and 158 mA h g−1, respectively, indicating excellent rate capability. DFT calculations demonstrated that the S/O co-doping reduced the adsorption energy of K+ on the hard carbon, which eased the electrochemical reactions and consequently the capacity. The co-doping also reduced the structural deformation during the potassiation/depotassiation process, and thus improved the stability. In another report, co-doping hard carbon with S and N was found to increase K ion diffusion, leading to an enhancement of the rate capability.216 The recorded reversible capacity of the S/N doped carbon anode was 437 mA h g−1 at 0.1 A g−1, and the capacity retention rate was 75% after 3000 cycles at 2 A g−1. Furthermore, a unique flower-like S/N/O tri-doped hard carbon structure has also been recently tested for PIBs. A facile sublime sulfur-vapor vulcanization process was used to prepare the tri-doped carbon.217 The 3D structure could offer affluent surface areas to adsorb the K+ ions and also promote electron transport, while the highly reactive –N–Cx–S– species enhanced the pseudo-capacitive behavior for K ion storage. As a result, the reversible specific capacity was 423 mA h g−1 at a low current density of 0.05 A g−1. The tri-doped carbon electrode also showed good rate capability with 251 mA h g−1 achieved at 1 A g−1, and long cycling stability (362 mA h g−1 after 300 cycles at a current density of 0.5 A g−1). These results also showed that the heteroatom doping of carbon could be used as a strategy to enhance the performance of carbon-based electrodes in PIBs.
Most reported non-graphite carbon materials had shown good reversible capacities and rate performance because of their special porous structure, large specific surface, and defects. Since the precursors are composed of C elements and various heteroatoms, e.g. N, S, P, and O, it was possible to produce heteroatom doped carbon directly from the raw materials. Doping non-graphitic carbon materials provided active sites on the surface, facilitating the interaction between K+ and the anode. However, new strategies to address the low initial Coulombic efficiency and cycling stability issues are still needed for practical application in commercial PIBs. Meanwhile, the relationship between the compositions/structures/phases and electrochemical performances of non-graphite carbon materials should be further studied deeply, offering novel ideas to enhance their properties and make PIB energy storage technology practical.


3.1.3 Other intercalation-type anode materials. 
Other non-carbon layered metal compounds such as transition metal oxides,272,273 sulfides,274–277 selenides,278–285 and carbonitride286–288 have shown attractive features as alkali metal ion anodes. The layered intercalation compound K2Ti4O9 was first reported as an anode material for PIBs by Munichandraiah et al.289 The recorded actual specific capacity of K2Ti4O9 was relatively low (80 mA h g−1 at 80 mA g−1). K2Ti8O17 nanorods were subsequently reported as an anode material for PIBs, synthesized via a facile hydrothermal method.290 The lattice spacing of the (1 1 0) crystal layer is about 0.367 nm, which is slightly larger than that of graphite. The reversible specific capacity was recorded to be 181.5 mA h g−1 at 20 mA g−1. After 50 discharge/charge cycles at a current density of 20 mA g−1, the capacity retention rate was about 61%, while the capacity at 500 mA g−1 is only 44.2 mA h g−1. Recently, Wang et al.291 reported a carbon-coated K2Ti2O5 microsphere (S-KTO@C) with porous microstructure via a simple spray drying method. S-KTO@C showed an excellent reversible discharge capacity of 155.1 mA h g−1 at 16 mA g−1, good rate capability, and cycling stability with a capacity retention of 77.1% at 160 mA g−1 after 1000 cycles. The authors attributed the enhanced K+ intercalation and pseudocapacitive behavior to the carbon coating. Chen et al.292 designed carbon-coated VPO4-based materials with different morphologies via a thermal reduction process after intercalating isobutanol into layered VOPO4·2H2O. One of the produced morphologies, the flower-like VPO4, could deliver an outstanding capacity of 400 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1 with stable long cycling stability (263 mA h g−1 at 500 mA g−1) after 500 cycles. Chen et al.293 reported carbon-coated V2O3 hollow spheres prepared via a facile solvothermal reaction. The hollow structure microspheres and the outer carbon coating could bear the volumetric changes and improve the electronic conductivity. The electrode displayed a good reversible capacity of 330 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1 after 500 cycles with the combination of intercalation and a pseudocapacitive mechanism. Xu et al.294 introduced a titanium oxynitride, TiOxNy, nanoparticle/carbon composite from the Ti3C2Tx MXene as an anode material for PIBs. The composite electrode exhibited a stable capacity of 150 mA h g−1 at 0.2 A g−1 after 1250 cycles and impressive rate performance (72 mA h g−1 at 1.6 A g−1).
Polyanionic compounds have also been investigated as anode host materials for potassium ion storage. Xu et al.295 prepared KTi2(PO4)3 nanocubes by a hydrothermal method and a subsequent heat treatment step. After coating with a carbon layer using sugar as the source, the specific capacity was increased from 30 mA h g−1 to around 80 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at 64 mA g−1. Sun et al.296 developed carbon-coated KTiOPO4 with an open framework for PIBs. The electrode fabricated using C@KTiOPO4 could achieve a reversible capacity of 102 mA h g−1 at 5 mA g−1 and a long lifespan with a capacity retention of −77% over 200 cycles. In situ XRD, XPS, and DFT calculation results reveal a biphasic and solid solution reaction mechanism and a smaller lattice volume change (9.5%), which explains the enhanced electrochemical performance.
Metal sulfides, such as MoS2, SnS2, WS2, TiS2, ZnS, CoS, etc., have attracted much attention for applications in PIBs due to their high theoretical capacities. Ren et al.297 indicated that the K ions could intercalate into the MoS2 lattice and form hexagonal K0.4MoS2. Despite the large radius of K+, the intercalation with MoS2 is not very damaging to the chalcogenide crystals. The electrode fabricated using MoS2 exhibited good cycling stability with a capacity retention of 97.5% after 200 cycles at 20 mA g−1. When the value x in the compound KxMoS2 is higher than 0.4, the intercalated compounds are not stable, limiting the reversible capacity of practical PIBs. Li et al.298 designed a tubular MoS2–N/O co-doped carbon nanocomposite with an enlarged interlayer spacing of 0.92 nm. In addition, to improve the K+ diffusion, the tubular structure mitigated the mechanical strain during the intercalation of K+ ions. The N/O co-doped carbon played triple roles; first, it improved the electronic conductivity. Second, it buffered volume expansion. Third, it inhibited the dissolution of the active components and other side reactions. Therefore, the MoS2–carbon nanocomposite was able to deliver a reversible capacity of 220 mA h g−1 after 300 cycles at 250 mA g−1, and the capacity can remain at 176 mA h g−1 after 500 cycles at a higher current density of 1 A g−1. Liu et al.299 prepared a nanocomposite structure by anchoring nano-rose-like MoS2 on rGO nanosheets. The strong chemical bonds (e.g. Mo–C and Mo–O–C) between MoS2 and rGO provided structural stability during the potassiation/depotassiation processes. The composite anode delivered an excellent initial capacity of 438.5 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1 with a remarkable rate capability of 196.8 mA h g−1 at 2 A g−1 and outstanding cycling stability for 1000 cycles without obvious fading at 500 mA g−1. Further investigation suggested that the exceptional electrochemical performance is due to the contribution of both intercalation and conversion (via a four-electron-transfer process) mechanisms. Liu et al.300 proposed an assembly of ultrathin MoS2 nanosheets on a hollow tubular carbon skeleton's inner surface. The interlayer distance is tuned by intercalating organic molecules of various sizes. The hollow tubular structure and the expanded interlayer could facilitate the diffusion and reactions of the K+ ions, thereby delivering a high specific capacity of 148.5 mA h g−1 after 10000 cycles at 2 A g−1. Huang et al.301 designed a compact 1T-MoS2/graphene nanocomposite bulk by tightening the hydrogel using capillary tension forces. The K+ ions could be electrochemically stored in the compact aerogel via mixed intercalation and conversion mechanisms, with fast K+ diffusion. The aerogel anode's reversible specific capacities could reach 511 and 327 mA g−1 at 0.1 and 1 A g−1, respectively. Lei et al.302 proposed an unexpected intercalation-dominated process for potassium storage in the interlayer gap of layered WS2. The electrode exhibited a reversible capacity of 103 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at 100 mA g−1 with a decay rate of 0.07% per cycle. When the current density increased to 500 mA g−1, the electrode could still be cycled at least for 400 cycles. Our group recently developed an anode of ultrafine zinc sulfide (ZnS) nanorods nested in a tertiary hierarchical structure through a facile solvothermal-pyrolysis process.303 The designed nanoarchitecture successfully provided short diffusion paths for both K+ ions and electrons, ensured the formation of a stable SEI, and buffered the volume expansion during cycling. The ZnS-based anode delivered specific capacities of 330 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1 and 208 mA h g−1 at 500 mA g−1 for 100 and 300 cycles, respectively. Shu et al.304 designed a defect-rich TiS2 anode material by inducing functional cation defects on the surface via a thermal annealing method. The authors proposed the generated titanium vacancies (VTi) to weaken the microscopic stress and strain during the de/intercalation process and provide active sites to reduce the adsorption energy of K. The defective TiS2 anode could keep 63.56% of the initial reversible capacity after 450 cycles. Other mono-sulfides and quasi 2D layered sulfides, such as ReS2,305 CoS,306 Co9S8,307 FeS2,308 and K0.36(H2O)yWS2,309 were also synthesized and usually mixed with carbon materials to fabricate the active materials of PIB anodes, with promising electrochemical performance.
In addition to sulfides, metal selenides are another family of chalcogenides that have been tested as active anode materials for PIBs. Li et al.310 demonstrated a nanosized MoSe2@carbon composite prepared via a facile ion complexation induced method followed by a thermal selenization step. The MoSe2@carbon anode achieved high specific capacity (433 mA h g−1 at 0.1 A g−1), good cycling stability (267 mA g−1 with a capacity fading of 15.8% after 5000 cycles at 5 A g−1), and an excellent rate capability (169 mA g−1 at 5 A g−1). Zhang et al.311 implanted sheet-like MoSe2 in carbon nanofibers during the electrospinning process. The composite has high structural stability and fast de/intercalation of K+ ions, thereby displaying a high reversible capacity of 316 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at 100 mA g−1. Zhang et al.312 reported a flexible freestanding anode by embedding Co0.85Se@carbon nanoboxes in carbon nanofiber films via a MOF-engaged electrospinning method followed by carbonization and selenidization steps. When fabricated into an anode for PIBs, the Co0.85Se@carbon composite could deliver a capacity of 299 mA h g−1 after 400 cycles at 1 A g−1 and a capacity of 166 mA h g−1 at a high current density of 5 A g−1. We also designed a unique structure by embedding Co0.85Se quantum dots in a mesoporous carbon matrix,313 which also showed an outstanding K-storage performance with a high capacity of 402 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at 50 mA g−1. Du et al.314 synthesized a crystal-pillar-like CuSe material with a cubic phase assembled from nanosheets. The as-prepared electrode delivered a high specific capacity of 280 mA h g−1 at 5 A g−1 in PIBs without any obvious capacity decay after 340 cycles. In situ XRD and ex situ TEM results indicated that the ionic storage process contains both the K+ ion insertion reaction (K0.5CuSe phase) and conversion reaction (Cu and K2Se phases). Shu et al.315 employed commercial TiSe2 directly as an insertion-type anode for K ion storage and showed that the selenide could deliver good cyclability and excellent rate performance even without applying any nano design approaches. Interestingly, Shu et al., in their work, showed that K0.24TiSe2 forming in the first cycle is stable during further cycling, providing a rigid framework for a reversible insertion/extraction process. TiSe2 was used to fabricate a full cell with a PB cathode, and the cell showed excellent Coulombic efficiency and cyclability. Other selenides tested as anodes for PIBs include VSe2 nanosheets,316 a FeSe2/N-doped carbon composite,317 porous FeSe2@C nanofibers,318 NbSe2 sheets,319 and a graphene-wrapped coral-like NiSe2 composite.320
In order to improve the electrochemical performances of selenide based anodes, ternary chalcogenides were investigated recently. Wang et al.321 first reported a few-layer ternary Ta2NiSe5 flake with an interlayer spacing of 1.1 nm, expanded by a facile ion-intercalation-mediated exfoliation method with Mg2+ cations and NO3− anions. The Ta2NiSe5 electrode delivered superior K+ ion storage performances, such as a high specific capacity (315 mA h g−1 after 50 cycles at 50 mA g−1), excellent rate capability (121 mA h g−1 at 1 A g−1), and stable long-cycling performance with a capacity retention of 81.4% after 1100 cycles. The DFT calculations and experimental results elucidated that the enlarged interlayers with low diffusion barriers and large adsorption energy could result in fast intercalation and high-efficiency adsorption of K+ ions, contributing to the high-performance in PIBs. Huang et al.322 incorporated dual anionic vacancies in MoSSe arrays anchored on a carbon nanofiber membrane. They found that the anionic vacancies improve the adsorption of K+ ions when compared with vacancy-free MoSSe. The reversible capacity could reach 370.6 mA h g−1 after 60 discharge/charge cycles at 100 mA g−1. Since the 3D porous structure of the vacancy-rich MoSSe arrays could remit the volume expansion during cycling, high capacity retention could be achieved with 220.5 mA h g−1 kept after 1000 cycles at 500 mA g−1. Vacancy design for the optimization of K+ ion storage could also open a feasible route to develop potential anode materials for practical PIBs.
In recent studies, 2D layered MXenes, consisting of transition metal carbides and carbonitrides, have shown considerable electrochemical performances as anode materials in LIBs, SIBs, PIBs, and capacitors.323–328 Michael et al.329 prepared a fabricated electrode from Ti3CNTz with a specific capacity of 75 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at 20 mA g−1. Alshareef et al.330 designed a general acid/alkali treatment strategy to introduce more porosity into MXene materials. The resulting K-V2C anode displays an improved capacity of 195 mA h g−1 at a current density of 50 mA g−1. When the current density increased to 3 A g−1, the capacity could maintain 70 mA h g−1 over 48 cycles. Xu et al.331 fabricated an electrode based on 2D Ti3C2Tx MXene flakes combined with flexible hard carbon. The fabricated 3D architecture could effectively accommodate the volume expansion of hard carbon during the cycling process, delivering an enhanced capacity of 280.6 mA h g−1 at 30 mA g−1, rate capability (102.2 mA h g−1 at 500 mA g−1), and stable cycling performance. Yin et al.332 further designed a Ti3C2 MXene and N-rich porous carbon nanosheet hybrid for high-performance PIB anodes via an electrostatic attraction self-assembly method (Fig. 12a). The hybrid combined several advantages, including large specific surface area, abundant active sites, and a unique 3D porous interconnected structure. The electrode maintained a high reversible capacity of 358.4 mA h g−1 after 300 cycles at 100 mA g−1 (Fig. 12b), which is larger than both the Ti3C2 MXene and N-rich porous carbon nanosheets. The enlarged interlayer spacing can also facilitate the ionic/electronic transport and improved the tolerance toward volumetric changes during the fast charge/discharge process. Therefore, a durable rate capability (191.2 mA h g−1 at 2.0 A g−1) and long cycling stability (151.2 mA h g−1 at 2.0 A g−1 after 2000 cycles) were obtained (Fig. 12c). Further DFT calculations and ex situ XRD analysis, as shown in Fig. 12d–f, indicated that the electrochemical reaction mechanisms are a mix of enhanced interfacial K+ adsorption and optimized intercalation processes, including the reactions of 8C + xK+ + xe− ↔ KxC8 and Ti3C2Tx + yK+ + ye− ↔ KyTi3C2Tx. Wu et al.333 designed a black phosphorene@V2CTx MXene hybrid anode based on van der Waals interactions to accelerate the K+ transport and tolerate the cycling volume changes. The composite could achieve an outstanding reversible capacity of 593.6 mA h g−1 at 0.1 A g−1 and excellent cycling stability of 261 mA h g−1 with a retention ratio of 86% after 3000 cycles at 2.0 A g−1. Based on the remarkable synergetic effect, several layered MXene-based composite electrode materials, such as layered Ti3C2Tx MXene/MoS2 composites,334 carbon-coated MoSe2/Ti3C2Tx MXene hybrid nanosheets,335 3D Fe3C@porous graphite carbon core–shell structures,336 N-doped graphene/ReSe2/Ti3C2 MXene heterostructure frameworks,337 and mesoporous NbN/N-doped carbon hybrids,338 were also developed with enhanced electrochemical performances, suggesting a new way to design high-performance anode materials for K ion storage.
 Fig. 12  (a) Schematic of the preparation of the Ti3C2 MXene and N-rich porous carbon nanosheet (PDDA-NPCN/Ti3C2) hybrids; (c) K+ ion storage and (b) long cycling performances of the PDDA-NPCN/Ti3C2 hybrids at different current densities for several cycles; and (d) voltage profile, (e) corresponding ex situ XRD patterns, and (f) supercells for the PDDA-NPCN/Ti3C2 anode at different stages during the initial potassiation/depotassiation process. 
Layered organic materials that can run on an intercalation process basis in alkali metal ion batteries have several advantages over their inorganic counterparts, including low toxicity, low cost, abundant sources, and controllable chemical structure.339 For example, a layered organic dipotassium terephthalate (K2TP) crystal with a cyclic moiety and conjugated structure for alkali metal ion storage was employed as the anode material for PIBs. Chen et al.340 reported that K2TP had enhanced electrochemical performance in 1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME) based electrolyte. Benefiting from the synergistic effect of abundant active carboxylate groups and a stable SEI, the reversible capacity achieved was 249 mA h g−1 at 200 mA g−1, and the capacity could maintain 94.6% of the initial value after 500 cycles at 1 A g−1. After replacing the adjacent carbon close to the ester group with nitrogen, superior electrochemical potassium storage performances could also be achieved in carbonate electrolyte.341 3,4,9,10-perylenetetracarboxylic diimide (PTCDI)342 was also tested as a high-rate anode for PIBs, benefiting from the low charge-transfer resistance and fast K ion diffusion. The PTCDI electrode could deliver a high specific capacity of 208 mA h g−1 with only 20 mA h g−1 capacity loss after 320 cycles at a current density of 5 A g−1. IR and XRD results suggested a six-electron storage mechanism for the PTCDI anode during the K-ion insertion/extraction process. Sun et al.343 developed an anode material based on a few-layer boronic ester-based covalent organic framework (COF) material anchored on CNTs. This novel organic material exhibited outstanding reversible capacities of 288 mA h g−1 after 500 cycles at 0.1 A g−1 and 161 mA h g−1 after 4000 cycles at 1 A g−1. The authors attributed the enhanced performance to the improved π-K+ interaction between the potassium ions and conjugated π-electrons of benzene rings. Similar conjugated microporous polymers (CMPs) with π-conjugated skeletons were also employed as anodes for PIBs.344 Jiang et al.345 studied the relationships of the structure and the performances of CMPs with the delocalized lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) distribution. By controlling the low LUMO energy level and narrowing the bandgap, along with the porous structure of CMPs, the electron conductivity, the number of active sites, and the accommodation of volume change during K intercalation could be enhanced. As a result, a reversible capacity as high as 428 mA h g−1 at 30 mA g−1 with excellent cycling stability (272 mA h g−1 after 500 cycles at 50 mA g−1) could be achieved. These examples could give promising green organic electrode candidates with large capacity and long cycling stability for PIBs. However, the poor electrical conductivity and the solubility of the organic materials in the electrolyte still limit their practical application in PIBs, as they do with other metal-ion batteries. Therefore, more effort is required to address these issues in future studies.

3.2 Conversion-type anodes
In conversion type anodes, the active material reacts with the alkali metal ions from the electrolyte to change the oxidation state of at least one of the metals in the anode. As a result, new compounds usually generated from the alkali-ions interstation. Because the total reduction of transition metal ions is usually considered up to the metallic state during electrochemical reactions, the theoretical capacities of conversion anode materials are always much higher than those of intercalation anodes. The following formula describes the general reversible reaction mechanism of conversion reactions in PIBs: MxAy + (yn)K+ + (yn) e−↔ xM + yKnA(8)where M is the transition metal (Fe, Co, Mo, etc.), A is the anion (O, S, etc.), and n is the oxidation state of A. Recently reported conversion anodes for PIBs included metal oxides and transition metal dichalcogenides.

3.2.1 Metal oxides. 
Several transition metal oxides have been considered promising PIB anode materials because of the high reversibility of the electrochemical conversion process. Jiao and co-workers346 synthesized CuO nanoplates with a thickness of around 20 nm as a high-performance anode. The theoretical reversible capacity of the anode, based on converting the formed Cu2O to Cu, is calculated to be 374 mA h g−1. Benefiting from the high surface area resulting from the ultrathin thickness, the CuO nanoplates afford a short K+ ion diffusion distance and large electrode–electrolyte contact interface. As a result, the electrode showed a high reversible capacity of 342.5 mA h g−1 at 200 mA g−1 (about 92% of the theoretical value). The CuO nanoplate-based anode also showed high cyclic stability; the capacity could remain over 206 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at a high current density of 1 A g−1. Kim et al.347 reported a Magnéli phase Ti6O11/CNT composite electrode through controlled electrochemical reduction of TiO2. The Magnéli phase anode exhibited a reversible capacity of ∼150 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1. The XRD and TEM analysis proved that the K storage is through a conversion reaction mechanism. Different from a simple oxide, Liang et al.348 applied a β-FeOOH/conductive carbon composite as a new anode for PIBs. The electrode could still deliver a high reversible capacity of 177.7 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1 after 100 cycles. The potassium storage mechanism was determined to start by converting β-FeOOH irreversibly into an amorphous structure. The amorphous phase remains stable in the subsequent cycles. Nithya et al.349 fabricated a MnCO3 nanorod@rGO composite via a simple one-step hydrothermal method without any further heat treatment. The electrode has a high surface area of 122.6 m2 g−1 because of a combination of the nano-sized MnCO3 nanorods with a diameter of 5–10 nm and the rGO flakes, facilitating the conversion reaction. The composite delivered a high capacity and superior long-term cycling stability (841 mA h g−1 after 500 cycles at 200 mA g−1), as an anode material for PIBs. The conversation reaction, MnCO3 + 2K+ + 2e− ↔ Mn + K2CO3, contributed mostly to the outstanding performances. Sultana et al.350 studied the potassium storage behavior of transition metal oxide anodes with the aim to mitigate the damaging volumetric changes and stress usually associated with conversion reactions. Then they prepared a Co3O4–F2O3/C nanocomposite via a molten salt method. The electrochemical characterization proved the new composite's ability to mitigate the structural damage problems of the conversion reactions. Compared with the Co3O4–F2O3 nanoparticles without carbon, the Co3O4–F2O3/C composite, with its higher specific surface area and conductivity, presented a reversible capacity of 220 mA h g−1 after 50 cycles at 50 mA g−1. In situ XRD results further indicate that the main potassium storage mechanism is the conversion of Co3O4 and F2O3 into their metallic states, according to Fe2O3 + 6K+ + 6e− ↔ 2Fe + 3K2O and Co3O4 + 8K+ + 8e− ↔ 3Co + 4K2O. These studies could offer meaningful strategies for developing high performance anodes for PIBs using earth-abundant transition metals, which is useful for commercial application.


3.2.2 Metal dichalcogenides. 
Conversion-based transition metal dichalcogenides, particularly sulfides, have also been considered for PIB anodes.351–354 Metal sulfides have good electrical conductivity and electrochemical activity. However, pure metal sulfides suffer from low cycle life due to transformation and conversion reactions. Researchers have tried to solve this problem by making these metal sulfides into composite systems with carbon, obtaining a great improvement in the electrochemical performances. One example is a carbon-coated Co3O4 and MoS2 composite electrode (Co3O4@C@MoS2) with a designed layered structure to accommodate the volume expansion.355 The composite anode delivered a specific capacity of 256 mA h g−1 with a capacity retention of 88.3% after 500 cycles at 500 mA g−1. Xie et al.356 used rGO as a carrier to stabilize MoS2 in a rose shape. The electrode has ultra-high potassium storage-specific capacities of 679 mA h g−1 and 178 mA h g−1 at 20 mA g−1 and 500 mA g−1, respectively. After 100 cycles under a current density of 100 mA g−1, the specific capacity was still above 380 mA h g−1. Interestingly, the authors suggested that the K+ ion storage mechanism of MoS2@rGO undergoes both the intercalation reaction and conversion reactions through in situ Raman results, according to MoS2 + xK+ + xe− ↔ KxMoS2 (above 0.54 vs. K/K+) and KxMoS2 + (4−x) K+ + (4−x) e− ↔ Mo + K2S (below 0.54 vs. K/K+).
Growing nanosized SnS2 and Sb2S3 directly on rGO has also been investigated to improve the electrochemical performance of metal sulfide conversion anodes.357,358 Although the specific capacity has improved compared to the pure SnS2 and Sb2S3, the cycling stability still needs to be improved. The cyclic stability could be enhanced by S/N co-doping of rGO. The capacity retention rate of SnS2–rGO was about 70% after 30 cycles at 25 mA g−1, which increased to about 89% after 100 cycles at 50 mA g−1 after the co-doping. In another attempt to accommodate the structural damage caused by potassiation/depotassiation reactions, Guo et al.359 exfoliated bulk layered Sb2S3 into a 2D form with a thickness of 6.8 nm using a high-shear mixer in water and ethanol. Ethanol was also used as a source of carbon, and they finally obtained the Sb2S3/C anode material for PIBs. Compared to the bulk Sb2S3, the 2D Sb2S3 nanosheets can effectively accommodate the changes during the electrochemical reactions. As a result, the Sb2S3/C electrode displayed enhanced cyclic stability with a reversible specific capacity of 404 mA h g−1 after 200 cycles at 500 mA g−1. The electrode also showed good rate performance with a capacity retention rate of about 76% when the current density increased from 50 to 500 mA g−1. Phases such as K3Sb and K2S3 could be detected during the charge/discharge process, suggesting that Sb2S3/C undergoes the intercalation reaction, in addition to the conversion reactions.
Since the nanosized metal sulfide particles are supported on graphene, the nanoparticles might also aggregate, reducing the surface available for the electrolyte/electrode interface and consequently the electrochemical performance. To address this issue, Wu et al.360 developed a “gel-blowing” method to confine FeS2 in an N-doped carbon matrix (Fig. 13a and b). The external carbon shell on the FeS2 nanoparticles can suppress their aggregation and enhance the transfer kinetics. The as-prepared FeS2@NC displayed a high specific capacity of 525.5 mA h g−1 at 0.1 A g−1 and a record long-term cyclability with 0.016% capacity loss per cycle over 5000 cycles (Fig. 13d). When the current density was increased to high rates of 5 and 10 A g−1, the anode also showed excellent rate performance with capacities of 236 and 155 mA h g−1, respectively (Fig. 13c). This study also systematically studied the reaction mechanisms during K storage, demonstrating a combination of intercalation and phase transformation. Similarly, Zhao et al.361 engineered a yolk–shell in which FeS2 nanoparticles on a graphene matrix were prevented from aggregating using a carbon coating film. Furthermore, Lu et al.362 synthesized hierarchical hybrid yolk–shell FeSe2@C microcapsules (Fig. 13e). Numerous electroactive FeSe2 nanoparticles were confined in the carbon shell, which avoided pulverization and alleviated volume expansion during repeat cycling. Besides, the external carbon shell also accelerated ionic migration and electron transmission (Fig. 13f). Yang et al.363 synthesized a FeP@C nanobox with a carbon shell (FeP@CNB) by phosphorizing a Fe2O3 nanobox yolk–shell structure as the precursor. The carbon shell improved the conductivity of the electrode, and the yolk–shell structure adapts to the volume expansion of FeP during the electrochemical process, thereby improving the cycling stability and rate performance. The FeP@CNB anode showed reversible capacities of 201, 156, 101, 65 and 37 mA h g−1 at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g−1, respectively. Our group designed an anode in which the FeP nanoparticles are incorporated in a 3D foam-like graphitic carbon scaffold (FeP@FGCS).364 FeP nanoparticles are homogeneously distributed and tightly fixed by the strong P–C bonds to the carbon scaffold. The designed FeP@FGCS structure provided a conductive pathway to accelerate the transport of K ions and electrons, and also alleviated the volume variation of FeP nanoparticles to preserve their integrity during charge/discharge. This study also showed that the selected ether-based electrolyte (1.0 M KFSI in DME) and binder promoted long cycling stability by forming a stable inorganic SEI layer on FeP@FGCS. Thus, the FeP@FGCS anode exhibited superior electrochemical performance with a high specific capacity of 382 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1, and a capacity of 183 mA h g−1 was retained for over 1000 cycles at 3 A g−1.
 Fig. 13  (a and b) FESEM images of FeS2@NC; (c) rate capability at various current densities ranging from 0.1 to 10 A g−1 and (d) long-term cycling stability of FeS2@NC; (e) HAADF–STEM image of yolk–shell FeSe2@C-3 MCs; and (f) illustration of the reaction mechanism of FeSe2@C-3 MCs in PIBs. 
NiS, as a member of the transition metal sulfide family, is rarely reported in PIBs due to the disadvantages of low conductivity and large volume expansion. By coating the precursor with a carbon film, Zhao et al.365 prepared a core–shell structure (NiS@C) to buffer the volume expansion during the potassiation/depotassiation cycles. The NiS@C anode could retain 171 mA h g−1 at 1 A g−1 after 300 cycles. On the other hand, nickel selenide NiSe2 has a low diffusion barrier (0.05 eV) and high carrier mobility (1658 cm2 V s), making it a better anode material than NiS, even without mixing with conductive materials. Wang et al.366 used 2D T-NiSe2 as an anode without the coating of hybridizing with carbon and showed that the initial electrode capacity could reach 247 mA h g−1 with better cycling stability than most 2D material based anode materials for PIBs.
Recently, copper sulfides, such as CuS and Cu2S, have been tested as conversion anodes for alkali metal ion batteries with impressive performance.367–371 Of particular interest, Cu2S has several properties that might benefit PIBs, including non-toxicity, low-cost, and high electrical conductivity. Yu et al.372 designed a uniform N-doped carbon-coated Cu2S hollow nanocube via an anion exchange process followed by PDA coating and carbonization steps (Fig. 14a–d). As shown in Fig. 14e, the composite nanocages have sufficient inner cavity to relieve the volume expansion during cycling. The high specific surface area shortens the diffusion pathway of potassium ions/electrons. The study also reported the formation of a stable SEI with lower interface impedance by using a high-concentration ether-based electrolyte (Fig. 14g). The Cu2S@NC anode showed excellent electrochemical performance with impressive reversible capacity and cyclic stability (372 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1 after 530 cycles, and 317 mA h g−1 at 1 A g−1 after 1200 cycles). The electrode also showed excellent rate performance (257 mA h g−1 at 6 A g−1) in a PIB half-cell (Fig. 14f). TEM and XRD analysis demonstrated a complex conversion reaction mechanism of the Cu2S@NC anode during the charge/discharge cycles.
 Fig. 14  (a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of Cu2S@NC; (b–d) SEM images of the Cu2O precursors, CuxS, and Cu2S@NC, respectively; (e) schematic of the degradation mechanisms of the Cu2S blocks and Cu2S@NC composite during the charge/discharge process; (f) cycling stability for the potassium-ion storage behavior of the Cu2S@NC anode at 100 mA g−1; and (g) cycling performance of the Cu2S@NC anode at 500 mA g−1 in different electrolytes. 
Zn-Based conversion anodes, e.g. ZnS, are very promising anodes for PIBs due to their advantages, including safety, non-toxicity, abundance, and low cost.373–376 In our recently published results, we designed an open ZnSe/C nanocage composed of sub-10 nm nanoparticles with a multi-hierarchy stress-buffering effect as anode materials for PIBs.377 The synergistic effect of all the ultrafine nanoparticles, the open structure, and the inner voids effectively reduced the diffusion-induced stresses and greatly enhanced the structural integrity. Meanwhile, the coated carbon layer could also improve the electronic conductivity and suppress ZnSe nanoparticle agglomeration. Owing to these merits, the ZnSe/C nanocages delivered a high reversible capacity of 318 mA h g−1 at 50 mA h g−1 as well as excellent cyclability up to 1000 cycles with a capacity of 189 mA h g−1 at 500 mA g−1. Various Fe-based anodes have also been evaluated for PIBs, such as hollow FexO nanospheres anchored on a 3D N-doped few-layer graphene framework,378 amorphous FeVO4,379 a 3D N-doped porous graphene framework decorated with Fe3O4 nanoparticles,380 3D carbon framework-supported FeSe,381 multicore-shell Fe2N nanoparticles impregnated into N-doped carbon nanofiber bundles,382 and a hierarchical Fe9S10@MoS2@C composite.383 The potassium ion storage performances of all these Fe-based electrodes were studied in detail, and many of them showed enhanced electrochemical performance.
Quantum dots, with their high specific surface area and reactivity, have also been used as energy storage materials.384–387 Gao et al.388 reported, for the first time, a CoS and GO composite as an anode for PIBs. Four samples with different GO contents (0, 10, 15, and 20%) were prepared by a two-step hydrothermal method by in situ vulcanizing the as-prepared Co(OH)2 nanosheets with GO. During the vulcanization process, the Co(OH)2 nanosheets transformed into CoS quantum dot nanoclusters in the surface of GO, exhibiting a larger surface area, high electrical conductivity, structural stability, and excellent electrochemical energy storage performance. At a current density of 500 mA g−1, the electrode displays a high specific capacity of 310.8 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles. Moreover, Li et al.389 reported a novel 3D structural self-supported NiCo2S4@N-C composite material by anchoring a tapered rod-shaped NiCo2S4 nanomaterial on N-doped hollow carbon nanofibers via a carbonization and hydrothermal method. The as-prepared electrode materials deliver a high capacity of 263.7 mA h g−1 after 200 cycles at 100 mA g−1 and outstanding stability with a capacity of 134.3 mA h g−1 after 600 cycles at 3.2 A g−1.
Generally, the reaction mechanisms of these aforementioned transition metal oxides and dichalcogenides are mostly based on the intercalation, conversion, and alloying process along with large volume changes during the potassiation/depotassiation reactions. As a result, these active materials’ aggregation and pulverization severely limit their rate performances and cycling stability. Most previous studies have tried to overcome these problems by engineering various nanostructures or mixing them with conductive carbon forming heterostructure composites. In both cases, the performance optimization is mainly attributed to the nanostructure and carbon's combined advantages, such as large contact area, abundant reaction sites, short diffusion path, high electronic conductivity, and adequate resilience to alleviate the volume changes and agglomeration during cycling. However, both the low initial CE from the largely irreversible capacity loss and the high and sloping operating voltage plateau hamper their practical applications in PIBs. Thus, future studies should focus more on understanding the relationship between the structures and the performances, consequently designing more efficient conversion electrodes for energy storage.


3.2.3 Other conversion anodes. 
MOFs are also considered potential anode candidates because of the possible polyelectron transfer by oxidizing/reducing both the inorganic and organic linkers. Other advantages that MOFs could offer are chemical composition control, porosity with controlled pore size, and high specific surface area. Therefore, MOFs could accelerate the diffusion rate of K+ ions and improve the electrolyte/electrode interaction. For example, Deng and co-workers390 reported a composite based on a low-cost microporous iron-based organic framework (MOF-235) mixed with CNTs as an anode material for PIBs. The composite electrode exhibited a specific capacity of 132 mA h g−1 over 200 cycles at 200 mA g−1 with remarkable rate capability. The enhanced electron conductivity from the carbon nanotubes and numerous active sites from the organic terephthalate moiety accounted for the high performance. Wang et al.391 proposed a non-redox-metal potassium MOF (K-MOF) as the active material and investigated the organic linker's redox reaction (pyridine dicarboxylate). The authors observed a reversible three-step redox reaction from [C7H3KNO4]n to [C7H3K2NO4]n accounting for a moderate average capacity of 115 mA h g−1 in 300 cycles at 100 mA g−1 with a capacity retention of 92%. Potassium perylene-3,4,9,10-tetracarboxylate (K4PTC)392 was also reported as a new two-electron redox-active organic anode for PIBs. Since the strong multiple K–O bonds could prevent the dissolution of the organic materials in the electrolyte, the K4PTC anode maintained a capacity of 100 mA h g−1 for 500 cycles at 50 mA g−1. Wang et al.393 investigated an organic azobenzene-4,4′-dicarboxylic acid potassium salt with an azo group as the redox center to reversibly react with K+ ions. They also claimed that a surface reaction-controlled mechanism controls the electrochemical storage. The electrode could deliver a high reversible capacity of 109 mA h g−1 over 400 cycles at 155 mA g−1. However, the reversible capacity and cycling stability of MOFs are still far from practical application. Thus, more effective strategies should be developed to enhance the electrochemical performances for promising electrode materials in PIBs.

3.3 Alloying anodes
Similar to LIBs and SIBs, Group IVA and VA elements (e.g. Si, P, Ge, Sn, Sb, Pb, Bi etc.) can alloy with potassium reversibly under an applied potential, allowing effective storage. These elements, and many of their metallic compounds (e.g., intermetallics, sulfides, and phosphides), have also attracted much attention due to the low operating voltage and high theoretical specific capacities, which aroused much interest in PIB applications.394–398 During potassium insertion, K-rich intermetallic compound(s) are formed, according to the following general reaction: xA + yK+ + ye− ↔ KyAx(9)where A is the alloying element, and KyAx is the final alloying reaction product. For instance, the final alloying product of Sb is K3Sb, which has a remarkable theoretical capacity of 660 mA h g−1, much higher than carbonaceous anode materials. However, like many other alloying-type anodes, Sb-based anodes suffer rapid capacity fading due to the large volume variations during charge/discharge. Moreover, new surfaces of active materials are always exposed after pulverization because of the huge volume changes. The SEI layers would reform each cycle, resulting in fast depreciation of the electrolyte and the anode materials. Recently, several practical strategies have been developed to overcome these problems, including designing special nanostructures, introducing a protective layer on the anode surface and employing suitable electrolytes. This section reviews the research progress related to alloy-based anodes and the scientific attempts to improve their electrochemical performance.

3.3.1 Tin anode. 
Tin (Sn) is an attractive alloy-based anode material for LIBs and SIBs due to the low cost and high theoretical capacities of 990 mA h g−1 (Li22Sn5 as the final phase) and 847 mA h g−1 (Na15Sn4 as the product), respectively.399 However, Sn anodes suffer significant volume change during cycling in both LIBs (420%) and SIBs (260%), which leads to severe structural pulverization and fast capacity fading. Similarly, the Sn anode presents a moderate theoretical capacity (226 mA h g−1), but the electrode also easily pulverizes upon potassiation/depotassiation reactions.400 Most reported research adopts nanostructure designs concepts to accommodate the sizeable volumetric change. One strategy is to buffer the volumetric change using nano-size Sn with a porous carbonaceous material. Sultana et al.401 prepared the Sn–C alloy by mechanical ball milling with a weight ratio of 7:3 in an Ar atmosphere. The alloy anode delivered a reversible specific capacity of about 150 mA h g−1. Huang and co-workers402 reported a 3D hierarchical Sn/porous carbon composite anode prepared via a high-temperature solid-state method using NaCl as the template. Sn nanoparticles with sizes in the range of 22–94 nm could homogeneously attach to the porous carbon matrix after carbonization at different temperatures. The reversible capacity could be optimized to be 276.4 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1 after 100 cycles at 650 °C. When the current density increased to 500 mA g−1, the capacity could still deliver 150 mA h g−1, suggesting high rate performance. In another study, Wang et al.403 encapsulated Sn particles in 3D porous rGO networks. The anode displayed a reversible capacity of 123.6 mA h g−1 after 500 cycles at 500 mA g−1 and an excellent rate capability of 67.1 mA h g−1 at 2000 mA g−1. Limiting the volume expansion by using Sn compounds instead of the pure metallic phase has also been investigated in the literature.404,405 Zhou and co-workers406 synthesized homogeneous SnO2 QDs with a size of 2–6 nm embedded in porous carbon via a hydrothermal method. Because of the well-restrained ultrasmall SnO2 QDs in a porous carbon matrix, the composite electrode could present an excellent reversible capacity of 300 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles. Even after 10000 cycles at a high current density of 1 A g−1, a considerable reversible capacity of 108.3 mA h g−1 could still be maintained. Xia et al.407 fabricated few layer SnS2 nanosheets on an rGO matrix, which presented a high specific capacity of 448 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1. The high capacity was attributed to both the conversion reaction (SnS2 to Sn) and alloying reactions (Sn to K4Sn23 and KSn) during the potassiation process. Along with fast K storage kinetics and excellent structure integrity, a stable long cycling performance with a capacity retention of 73% after 300 cycles was recorded. In order to further improve the attenuation of the SnS2-based anode capacity, Ci et al.408 coated SnS2 on porous rGO networks with an amorphous carbon film (SnS2@C@rGO). Since the two carbon-based layers restrained the SnS2 volume expansion, the volume expansion during the alloying process was significantly reduced, and the SnS2@C@rGO anode was able to deliver a high reversible capacity of 298.1 mA h g−1 and 170.9 mA h g−1 after 500 cycles at a current density of 0.2 and 0.5 A g−1, respectively. Despite all these efforts to enhance the Sn-based anode performance, the reversible capacity is still much lower than the theoretical capacity. More studies should focus on improving the capacity and CE of Sn-based electrodes in the future.


3.3.2 Antimony anode. 
Antimony (Sb) is also well known for its good conductivity and alloying ability with alkali metal elements. Sb could alloy with Li and Na to form Li3Sb and Na3Sb as the final product of the charging process with high capacities. Wang et al.409 investigated the intermediate phase during the potassiation process of the Sb anode by DFT calculations. In combination with CV measurements, they revealed a consequence of phase transformation from Sb to KSb2, KSb, K5Sb4, and finally to K3Sb with increasing potential, showing a high theoretical capacity of 660 mA h g−1. However, a considerable volume expansion of 407% was reported, resulting in rapid capacity fading. The authors further designed a Sb@CSN composite by encapsulating Sb nanoparticles in a carbon sphere network to tackle this issue. Using concentrated 4 M KTFSI/EC + DEC as the electrolyte, the Sb@CSN anode could deliver a remarkable capacity of 551 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at 100 mA g−1. For example, Lin et al.410 embedded Sb nanoparticles with an average size of 14.0 nm on the surface of 18.6 nm thick ultrathin carbon nanosheets by a one-step solvothermal “metathesis” chemical reaction. The composite anode displayed a reversible capacity of 247 mA h g−1 after 600 cycles at 200 mA g−1, equivalent to 90% of the initial capacity. Similar work using a carbon matrix to buffer Sb-based anodes’ volumetric changes has been reported in the literature with improved electrochemical properties,411 such as a 3D macroporous Sb@carbon composite412 and segment-like Sb nanorod encapsulated in an N-doped hollow carbon tube.413 Moreover, Sb nanoparticles are also incorporated with conductive and flexible MXene paper to form a freestanding flexible MXene@Sb anode.414 MXene@Sb delivered a high reversible capacity of 516.8 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1 and a stable long-term cycling capability with a capacity fading rate of only 0.042% per cycle. Ou et al.415 impregnated Sb nanoparticles into few-layer MoS2 nanosheets and then encapsulated the MoS2/Sb heterostructure into an N-doped graphene framework as a complex anode material for PIBs. Due to the MoS2 nanosheets and N-doped graphene's synergistic effect in the chrysanthemum-like structure, the hierarchical composite electrode could prevent the coarsening of alloyed Sb nanograins, alleviating the volume expansion during de/potassiation, and promoting the electron/ion conductivity. As a result, a high reversible capacity of 359.5 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1 and outstanding cycling stability with a reversible capacity of 170.1 mA h g−1 over 1000 cycles at 2 A g−1 were reported for the MoS2/Sb/N-graphene anode. Another strategy to alleviate the volumetric changes during de/potassiation is by alloying Sb with other metals to form intermetallic compounds.416 Xu et al.417 reported a nanosheet composite by embedding Bi–Sb alloy nanoparticles in a porous carbon matrix via a freeze-drying and pyrolysis method. The Bi and carbon can effectively buffer the volume change during the charge/discharge process, delivering a reversible capacity of 320 mA h g−1 at 500 mA g−1 over 600 cycles from the reversible alloying reaction mechanism of (Bi,Sb) ↔ K(Bi,Sb) ↔ K3(Bi,Sb). When the as-prepared composite was used to assemble a full cell with PB as the cathode, it can still deliver a high capacity of 360 mA h g−1 after 70 cycles. Besides, Sb-based chalcogenides, e.g. Sb2Se3, were also developed as stable two-step conversion/alloying anodes for PIBs.418 When combined with porous carbon nanomaterials (such as rGO and N-doped carbon layers), the volume expansion of Sb2Se3 nanoparticles could be relieved, thereby enhancing the reversible specific capacity and stability. Another Sb compound, the bimetallic oxide Sb2MoO6, was investigated to improve the performance of Sb anodes. Sb2MoO6 anchored on rGO is further reported as a PIB anode material.419 Lu et al. reported that the Sb2MoO6/rGO anode has a discharge capacity of 402 mA h g−1 at 100 mA g−1, and the capacity can still keep stable at 247 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles when the current density increases to 100 mA g−1. In situ XRD, TEM, and DFT calculations indicated that most of the high capacity is from the alloying/dealloying of Sb, which stems from the irreversible reduction of Sb2MoO6. Meanwhile, amorphous Mo, resulting after the first cycle, could improve the electrode's conductivity and relieve the volume change of Sb during cycling. Although different methods have been proposed to enhance the Sb anode's electrochemical performances, the cyclic stability and initial CE are still unsatisfactory. Future research should focus more on the fundamentals of capacity fading and improvement of the cyclic stability and rate performance for Sb-based PIB anodes.


3.3.3 Bismuth anode. 
Similar to Sn and Sb, bismuth (Bi) is a promising metal anode for PIBs, which can also alloy with three K ions due to its unique layered crystal structure with a large interlayer spacing of 3.95 Å. Although pure metallic Bi electrodes could deliver a higher theoretical capacity of 385 mA h g−1 (based on the formation of K3Bi), Bi-based electrodes still attract little attention in PIBs. The main reason might be their low CE and fast capacity fading. Guo et al.420 reported that the de/alloying process consists of two typical multi-phase reactions to form K3Bi in the initial potassiation process and a reversible stepwise mechanism (K3Bi ↔ K3Bi2 ↔ KBi2 ↔ Bi) in the following de/potassiation cycles. The total volume expansion of the phase transformation from Bi to K3Bi was found to be as high as 406%, which is much larger than that of Na3Bi (≈244%) and Li3Bi (≈106%). Thus, nanostructural design approaches, such as 2D few-layer bismuthene,421 have been proposed to improve PIB anodes’ performances. Engineering nanostructured Bi-based anodes can promote fast ion diffusion, facile electrolyte penetration, and the ability to buffer the volume change along the c-axis. The bismuthene anode showed excellent rate performance with the capacity reaching values of 423, 356, 275, and 227 mA h g−1 at 2.5, 5, 10, and 15 A g−1, respectively. Long-term cycling stability with a capacity of 200 mA h g−1 is obtained at 2 A g−1. In addition to nanostructural design, introducing various carbon matrixes to host Bi particles has also been proven to be an efficient way to accommodate the huge volume expansion during the alloying/dealloying process. Tai and co-workers422 developed a hierarchical bismuth nanodot/graphene composite by facile in situ and spontaneous reduction. Bi nanodots with a size of 3 nm are well confined between the graphene layers, preventing them from aggregating. The composite anode showed great ability to accommodate the volume change in addition to providing ionic transport channels. As a result, the Bi-nanodot/graphene composite electrode displayed an impressive reversible capacity of 213 mA h g−1 after 500 cycles at 5 A g−1. It even provided a stable rate capability of 200 mA h g−1 at 10 A g−1. Qu et al.423 fabricated hollow Bi@N-doped C nanorods via a simple evaporation method. In this structure, the hollow nanorods can buffer the volume changes of Bi during de/potassiation cycles, and the outer conductive carbon shells could promote electron transfer. A reversible capacity of 353 mA h g−1 can be achieved at 50 mA g−1, and the capacity retention is 80.3% after 300 cycles at 500 mA g−1. Jian et al.424 reported hollow structure N-doped carbon-coated Bi nanorods, which also exhibited a high-rate capability and excellent long-term cycling performance. Other Bi-based compounds that can combine both the conversion and alloying reactions, such as chalcogenides and oxyhalides, were also employed as high-performance PIB anodes. Neeraj et al.398 reported a tetradymite-type Bi2Te3 anode for rechargeable Li, Na and K-ion batteries, which exhibited initial discharge capacities of 480, 498, and 456 mA h g−1 in the three battery systems, respectively. Similar to the binary oxide Sb2MoO6, nanoplate-assembled Bi2MoO6 microspheres were tested as an anode for high-performance PIBs by Zhang et al.425 Without hybridizing with conductive carbon materials, the Bi2MoO6 microspheres could still deliver a stable reversible capacity of 121.7 mA h g−1 over 600 cycles at 100 mA g−1. Lei et al.426 found that layered bismuth oxyhalides, which are promising photocatalysts, could also be used as anodes in PIBs. The as-prepared BiOCl nanoflakes delivered a high specific capacity of 367 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1 by forming K–Bi alloys during the potassiation reaction. Lu et al.427 attached bimetallic oxychloride Bi0.51Sb0.49OCl nanosheets on rGO nanosheets. The authors proposed a storage mechanism according to the following sequence: Bi0.51Sb0.49OCl → (Bi,Sb) ⇌ K(Bi,Sb) ⇌ K3(Bi,Sb). The BiOCl electrode exhibited low charge/discharge plateaus and a high discharge capacity of 360 mA h g−1 after running for 1000 cycles at 100 mA g−1. When the current density increased to 1 A g−1, the discharge capacity could remain as high as 319 mA h g−1.


3.3.4 Phosphorus anode. 
Phosphorus (P) can alloy with potassium to form K3P via a three-electron reaction. Along with its light weight, the P anode material exhibits the highest theoretical capacity of 2596 mA h g−1 for PIBs, as it did for LIBs and SIBs.428–431 There are several allotropes known for the P element. White phosphorus is probably less suitable for electrochemical energy storage because of its toxicity and flammability in the air. On the other hand, red phosphorus is stable but has low electronic conductivity, reducing the electrochemical redox reactions.432 The 2D allotrope, known as black phosphorus, possesses the highest stability and electron mobility of all the three allotropes. Both red and black phosphorus seem to be potential anode materials for PIBs. However, practical applications of these pure P-based materials are hard to achieve since their reactivity significantly reduces the initial CE capacity, limiting the battery life to a few cycles. Thus, protocols have been employed in the literature to address these issues. One of these strategies is introducing carbon materials to improve their intrinsic insulating electronic performance and optimize their cycling stability. Sultana et al.433 found that, when encapsulating pure black phosphorus in a carbon matrix, the cycling performance is related to the mass ratio of black phosphorus to carbon. When the mass ratio of black phosphorous to carbon is 7:3, the specific capacity drops to nearly 0 mA h g−1 after 50 cycles at 50 mA g−1. When the mass ratio is 1:1, the composite exhibits a specific capacity of 270 mA h g−1 after 50 cycles under the same current density, and the capacity retention rate is 60%. In a recently published paper, Yu and co-workers434 reported an outstanding PIB anode by embedding red phosphorus into N-doped porous hollow carbon nanofibers (red P@N-PHCNF) via a vaporization–condensation method (Fig. 15a). The SEM and TEM images indicated that the tubular structured composites exhibited an amorphous characteristic without any red P nanoparticles on the carbon nanofiber's surface (Fig. 15b–d). The special structure could buffer the volume expansion. The results showed a strong P–C bond, and P atoms are adsorbed at N-doped sites, facilitating the transfer and diffusion of K+ ions (Fig. 15e and f). The rationally designed electrode delivers an ultra-high reversible specific capacity of 650 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at a low current density of 100 mA g−1 (Fig. 15g). After 800 cycles at a high current density of 2 A g−1, the specific capacity was still maintained at 465 mA h g−1 (Fig. 15h). In addition, the composite anode also exhibited an excellent rate performance with a capacity of 342 mA h g−1 at 5 A g−1 (Fig. 15h). In situ Raman and ex situ XRD results confirmed that the final discharge product of red P in this composite is K4P3 according to the overall reversible reaction 4K + 3P ⇆ K4P3. Tuan et al.435 used the ball-milling method to prepare a red P/multiwall CNT/Ketjenblack composite material. The obtained reversible capacity (750 mA h g−1), rate performance (300 mA h g−1 at 1 A g−1), and cycling life (680 cycles) were slightly lower than the aforementioned vaporization–condensation method. Qu et al.436 coated a P-activated carbon composite with a thin polypyrrole layer, producing air-stable red P, and protecting the active materials from severe reaction with the electrolyte. The PPy-coated P@AC electrode has a P content of −52 wt% and delivered a capacity of 400 mA h g−1 at 20 mA g−1. In addition, forming metal phosphides was proved to increase the stability of the electrode. Examples of metal phosphides tested for PIBs include hierarchically porous carbon-supported Sn4P3,437 trigonal SnP@C,438 a SnP3/C nanocomposite,439 yolk–shell structured FeP@C nanoboxes,440 MoP@N/P-codoped carbon nanofibers,441 and a Se3P4@mesoporous carbon composite.442 Among these compounds, the Se3P4@C composite obtained excellent electrochemical performance with a remarkable initial capacity of 1036.8 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1.442 The CE of the first cycle was 68.9%, but it quickly increased to 97.5% and stabilized at about 99.3% in the following cycles. Se3P4@C showed exceptional cycling stability with a capacity retention of 78.9% after 300 cycles at 1000 mA g−1. The authors suggested that the high capacity was due to the reversible potassiation process Se3P4 + (18 − 4x)K+ + (18 − 4x)e− ⇌ 4K3−xP + 3K2Se, and the synergistic effect between Se and P elements that provides a buffering effect to reduce the volume change and retain the cycling stability. As can be concluded from the published work in this area, the capacity is proportional to the P content in the electrode, while the cycling performance is inversely proportional to the P loading in the composite. Thus, it isn’t easy to find an appropriate balance between the capacity and cyclic stability in PIBs. In future work, the main direction for P-based anodes is still the optimization of the electrochemical performances.
 Fig. 15  (a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis process of the red P@N-PHCNF sample; (b–d) the SEM, TEM, and HRTEM images of the red P@N-PHCNF; (e and f) the de/potassiation process and charge transfer in the red P@N-PHCNF sample; and (g and h) cycling performance at 0.1 A g−1, 2 A g−1, and 5 A g−1, respectively. 


3.3.5 Optimizing alloy anodes. 
In addition to optimizing the active materials, the rational use of electrolytes and binders is a practical approach to maximize alloy anodes’ performance. As reviewed previously, the anode materials, either pure metals or in the form of compounds, have a large volume expansion during potassiation. For example, the Sb and Bi-metal expansion rate during the reaction is about 300% and 406%, respectively, leading to a poor cycling performance in PIBs. By choosing potassium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (KFSI) to replace potassium hexafluorophosphate (KPF6) in an EC and DEC (1:1 volume) mixture, Guo et al.420 reported that the Bi/rGO anode could deliver a higher initial charge capacity of 441 mA h g−1 and maintained a more stable reversible capacity of 290 mA h g−1 after 50 cycles in KFSI electrolyte. Further studies indicate that the FSI− anion could inhibit the electrolyte decomposition, modify the surface passivation, and form a more uniform, conductive, stable, and robust SEI, thereby improving the cycling stability. The KFSI salt is also very versatile and can significantly promote the electrochemical performance of other alloy-based anode materials, such as Sn/C and Sb/C composites. Furthermore, the above-mentioned results by Lei and Huang et al.443,444 are based on the non-carbonate electrolyte DME. The interaction between DME molecules and Bi in this electrolyte promotes the potassiation/depotassiation cycling. A 3D structure might be formed during the alloying process, which could also improve the cycling performance of the anode in PIBs. These electrolytes and binders can promote the ion transfer and enhance electronic contact within the active material and with the current collector. Future work should focus on electrolyte and binder optimization for improving alloy-type anodes’ performance.

4. Common synthesis methods of electrode materials
