The synthesis methods are essential for the practical application of PIB electrode materials.445,446 Many pieces of research have been carried out to develop the relationship between the synthesis methods, morphology, structure and properties of materials.447–451 Furthermore, adjusting the synthesis parameters to control the electrode materials’ microstructure and morphology could provide the base for more suitable electrode materials with enhanced performance.446 Several approaches are used in the literature to prepare active materials for PIB electrodes, including co-precipitation, hydrothermal, solvothermal, high temperature solid-state, sol–gel, spray pyrolysis, mechanical ball milling and electrodeposition methods.45,46,57 There are also studies on combining two or more of these methods to optimize the electrode structure and performance.33 This section summarizes common synthesis methods used to prepare PIB electrode materials with critical views on their advantages and disadvantages for their commercial application.33,45
4.1 Co-precipitation method
The co-precipitation method is a common strategy for preparing electrode materials for several energy storage applications.452 In this method, metal salts dissolve in water and then react with preparation agents, forming water-insoluble compounds. Therefore, the co-precipitation method is straightforward, with a short synthesis period.453 Controlling the morphology and crystallinity of the final product is possible via controlling the precipitation reaction conditions such as the pH, the precipitation agent, and the solution temperature, or even using directing agents or templates during the precipitation.86 Concerning PIBs, the co-precipitation methods are mostly used to synthesize cathode materials, such as PB and PBAs,62,63 layered metal oxides,73 and chalcogenides.57 For example, PBA nanoparticles were prepared by dropping FeCl3 solution into K4Fe(CN)6 solution, which exhibited a relatively irregular spherical morphology with a crystal grain size of 20–30 nm.78 When dropping dilute HCl solution into K4Fe(CN)6 solution at 60 °C, the PBA morphology changed into regular cubic nanoparticles with size ranging from 500 to 700 nm.82 Further analysis showed that the cubic PBAs have better crystallinity than the irregular spherical particles produced without HCl addition, which was beneficial for the high specific capacity and cycling stability of cubic PBAs.
However, co-precipitation methods suffer from several drawbacks. First, the as-prepared grains’ composition might be uneven in different regions due to the partial high concentration distributed in mixed solutions.453 Also, the crystallinity of the product obtained by the co-precipitation method is commonly low; thus, additional processes, such as further heat treatment, are usually required to optimize the material's crystallinity. Therefore, the co-precipitation reaction conditions should be strictly controlled to ensure the electrode material's quality for large-scale applications.452
4.2 Hydrothermal and solvothermal methods
Hydrothermal and solvothermal processes are based on chemical reactions at high temperature and pressure in water/organic solvents, usually in a sealed pressure vessel.453 These methods have been widely used for preparing PIB cathode materials, such as layered metal oxides and polyanionic compounds.113,133 Deng et al.115 reported the preparation of homogeneous layered P2 type K0.65Fe0.5Mn0.5O2 microspheres with a diameter around 100 nm, using urea, ascorbic acid, and polyvinylpyrrolidone additives as hydrothermal raw materials. When they acted as the cathode materials for PIBs, the microspheres could provide good electronic conductivity, a shortened ion diffusion distance, and an optimized contact area between the cathode material and electrolyte, as well as superior structural robustness against strain and stress induced by K+ ion intercalation/deintercalation. As a result, excellent electrochemical performances were obtained. KTi2(PO4)3 nanocubes were also synthesized with a 200–300 nm diameter starting from anatase TiO2, KH2PO4·2H2O, and phosphoric acid as the precursors for the hydrothermal process. The nanocubes coated with carbon showed a stable structure and performed an efficient potassium storage reaction.295
In addition, hydrothermal and solvothermal methods have also been used to prepare PIB anode materials, such as various nanostructured oxides,346 sulfides,389 selenides,377 and their complexes.349 For example, CuO nanoplates with a diameter of 20 nm were synthesized via a simple hydrothermal process starting from Cu(NO3)·3H2O. The prepared nanoplates were of good crystallinity and possessed a moderate surface area (48.5 m2 g−1) with abundant reaction sites.346 The well-designed structure could be conducive to electron transfer and K+ diffusion, which further resulted in a reversible capacity of 342.5 mA h g−1 at 0.2 A g−1. Moreover, Nithya et al.349 developed a MnCO3 nanorod@rGO composite anode with a high specific surface area of 122.6 m2 g−1, where the 5–10 nm size MnCO3 nanorods are stably fixed on the surface of rGO sheets. The composite, used as a PIB anode, could provide both high reversible capacity and superior long-term cycling performance.
Compared with other methods, the particles prepared by the hydrothermal or solvothermal method are uniform in size and can be controlled to the nanoscale. Besides, the electrode material morphology can also be effectively controlled by adjusting the reaction conditions, such as the reaction time, temperature, pH, and additives.453 However, hydrothermal or solvothermal methods are susceptible to the reaction conditions and the equipment used. Also, the methods’ productivity is low, which leads to more challenges for commercial mass production.454
4.3 High-temperature solid-state reaction method
The high-temperature solid-state reaction method is a common method for ceramic material synthesis and has been adopted to produce several structures suitable for PIB electrodes.453 Due to the high-temperature nature and good crystallinity of the product, this method is usually applied for layered metal oxide cathode materials.48 Zhang et al.99 prepared a P2-K0.44Ni0.22Mn0.78O2 cathode material by heating a stoichiometric amount of K2CO3, NiO, and MnO2 at 1000 °C for 30 h. XRD analysis proved that the product is of high crystallinity and purity with only 3% of the NiO impurity, facilitating the de/intercalation processes. The full cell fabricated using this P2-K0.44Ni0.22Mn0.78O2 cathode and a soft carbon anode showed excellent potassium storage performances. The capacity retention rate could be as high as 90% after 500 cycles. Liu et al.101 starting from oxides also synthesized a series of iron-substituted layered P3 manganese oxide K0.45Mn1−xFexO2 cathodes, which showed outstanding cycling stability and rate performance when x = 0.2. The authors also indicated that these properties could be further improved by regulating their particle size, porosity, and surface morphologies under different synthesis conditions.48
High-temperature solid-state processes have also been used to prepare anode materials, such as carbon composite materials, and metal oxide materials.44,45,57 For example, Ju et al.197 reported few-layer fluorinated graphene foam (FFGF) with a 4 nm thickness and high surface area (874 m2 g−1) by heating polyvinylidene fluoride at high temperatures in an inert atmosphere. After 50 cycles at a current density of 100 mA g−1, the capacity of FFGF could maintain 800 mA h g−1 with excellent rate performance. Kishore et al.289 developed a K2Ti4O9 anode by calcining a stoichiometric amount of K2CO3 and TiO2 at 900 °C for PIB research.
Although solid-state methods are simple and easy to scale up, they suffer several drawbacks that hinder their wide applications. One of these drawbacks is the large particle size of the produced materials, which decreases the electrode ability to resist pulverization upon cycling. During solid-state heating, it is easy for the active materials to get contaminated by various impurities, thus damaging their electrochemical performance. Finally, many of these solid-state methods require heating for a long time at high temperature, making them energy-intensive processes.453
4.4 Sol–gel method
Similar to solid-state methods, the sol–gel process is adopted from the ceramics industry. The process involves forming a solid network through polycondensation reactions of a molecular precursor in a liquid. To enhance the crystallinity of the final materials, the sol–gel process's green product is usually subjected to calcination at moderate temperatures.453 Zheng et al.130 reported a carbon-coated K3−xRbxV2(PO4)3/C cathode by the sol–gel method, using citric acid as a chelating agent followed by calcination at 850 °C. The synthesized samples presented good cyclic stability with a capacity retention ratio of 95.4% after 100 cycles. Chen et al.243 developed a hierarchical porous nitrogen-doped carbon microsphere (NCS) anode material derived from marine product waste via a sol–gel method. Based on the surface-driven potassium storage mechanism, the NCS electrodes showed an excellent rate of 154 mA h g−1 at an extremely high current density of 72 °C.
The advantages of the sol–gel method for PIB electrode materials could be summarized as a low reaction temperature, short reaction time, uniform particle size distribution, and small grain size.455 The sol–gel method's disadvantages might include the slightly complicated precursor, the product sensitivity to the precursor solution and drying/calcination conditions and the hardly controllable particle size growth during the calcination step.453,455
4.5 Spray drying method
The spray drying method produces a dry powder from a liquid or slurry by rapid drying using hot gas. Generally, a consistent particle size distribution could be obtained for industrial utilization.447 For example, a layered structure P2-K0.75[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 cathode material with a particle size of 2 µm was synthesized via a spray drying method by Jo et al.,100 showing a high specific discharge capacity. Zhao et al.291 reported the preparation of K2Ti2O5 microspheres (S-KTO) with an average diameter of approximately 12 µm by a similar method, using a mixture of TiO2, K2CO3, and C6H12O6 as the raw materials. A 10 nm carbon layer was then coated on the microsphere surface via a chemical vapor deposition process. The core–shell structure promoted fast electron transfer and improved the electrode material's structural stability, enhancing the K storage performances dramatically. Despite the clear advantages of the high productivity, spray drying suffers from several drawbacks, such as contaminating the materials during the process, large particle size, and the need for high temperature and a vacuum.447
4.6 Mechanical ball milling method
This process is based on grinding raw material mixtures into fine metal, alloy, compound, and composite powders, by the localized high pressure formed during the collision between tiny rigid balls.456,457 The process might involve phase transitions or chemical reactions induced by mechanical forces. Jo et al.106 obtained a K2V3O8/C composite cathode material by blending K2V3O8 powder with 5 wt% carbon black by high-energy ball milling for 12 h. Besides reducing the particle size, ball milling also helped induce defects in both the carbon and K2V3O8, introducing more active sites to initiate the electrochemical storage reactions. Consequently, the K2V3O8/C cathode delivered a reversible capacity of 75 mA h g−1, which could be retained for at least 200 cycles. Chang et al.435 reported a red phosphorus/carbon (RP/C) composite electrode by combining RP, MWCNTs, KB, NaCMC, and water through a one-pot ball milling process in a stainless steel tank. RP with an average size of about 200–500 nm is evenly distributed in the conductive frame constructed from MWCNTs and KB. XPS results showed no bond between P and C, limiting the alloying reactions between RP and K. The RP/C electrode exhibited a high reversible specific capacity of around 750 mA h g−1 and an excellent high-rate capability (≈300 mA h g−1 at 1000 mA g−1). In conclusion, the mechanical ball milling method has easy operation and low cost, but the particle distribution's purity and uniformity are hard to control.453,456
4.7 Electrodeposition method
By the electrodeposition method, solid materials can be deposited on the surface of conductive materials.458,459 Controlling the deposited materials’ morphology is possible by controlling the electrochemical properties, such as the applied potential, current density, electrolyte composition and the surface properties of the electrode. Tian et al.414 reported an MXene@Sb cathode material by depositing Sb nanoparticles on the surface of a hierarchical porous platform of Ti3C2Tx MXene. The Sb nanoparticles were deposited from SbCl3 solution in ethylene glycol under a constant density of 1 mA cm−2 using a Pt counter electrode. The stable structure could provide a short distance for K+ ion diffusion, and the porous structure would also buffer the volume expansion of the potassiation/depotassiation process. Finally, a high reversible capacity of 516.8 mA h g−1 and a stable capacity retention rate were obtained. In addition to the ability to control the morphology of the product, electrodeposition possessed several other advantages, such as the purity of the product, low energy consumption, low waste generation, and being easy to scale up. However, application of the method is limited to depositing metals or semi-metals on conductive substrates.
4.8 Other methods and future outlook
In addition to the methods mentioned above, there are also many other methods for preparing electrode materials of PIBs, such as the template method,460 solution immersion method,198 electrostatic spinning method,312 and various high-temperature carbonization methods.242,249,254,312 The electrochemical performances of the as-prepared materials via these methods might also be different. In future studies, optimizing the existing synthesis methods, developing novel synthesis methods, or designing a combination of other synthesis methods should be carried out to enhance the performances of electrode materials in PIBs and further promote their practical application in energy storage.
5. Full cell PIBs
5.1 The significance of the full cell test
At present, the vast majority of studies on PIBs, as with other alkali metal ion batteries, focus on either single cathode or anode materials, using metallic potassium as the counter and reference electrodes to evaluate the electrochemical performance and mechanism.47,57 As reviewed in the previous sections, the cathode materials exhibit a high working voltage compared to their LIB counterpart.82,107 We can also conclude that the anode materials exhibit greater cycling stability and higher rate performance on average than the cathode.208,234 PIBs are still facing several challenges, which are not limited to increasing the working voltage and specific capacity of half-cells, but, more importantly, include the question of what combination of materials could collaboratively work to ensure that the full cell meets the energy density requirements.33,45 It is still necessary to ensure full-cell economic sustainability to meet large-scale energy storage requirements. Therefore, we will further discuss the design and research of full cell PIBs in the following content.
5.2 Design of full cell PIBs
Full cell PIBs consist of a cathode, an anode, a separator, and an electrolyte, usually potassium salts in an organic solvent and sometimes with additives to realize an extra function. These components can be used to design different configurations of the full cell. In the lab, the components of a full-cell design are usually assembled in coin cell type devices. Examples of commonly used coin cells conforming to the relevant International Standard (IEC) are CR2025 and CR2032. These coin cell PIBs are well suited to powering small portable devices, such as LEDs. According to the literature results, we found that commonly reported cathode materials for full cell PIBs include PB, layered metal oxides, polyanionic compounds and organic materials.108,135,165 Their matched anode materials contain carbon-based materials, metallic alloy materials, and some other materials.218,423,435 It is worth noting that most cathodes and anodes of full cells often need a complete pre-potassiation process in the half-cell with metallic potassium as the counter electrode to eliminate the adverse effects in the initial cycle. Also, prior to assembly, the charge balance of the cathode and anode needs to be optimized by controlling the mass ratio.
Like LIBs, the operating voltage, cycling performances and high price of cathodes are the bottlenecks restricting their further practical applications.21 Compared with the anodes, cathode development is slightly slower since it has more technical problems.25 It is widely accepted that the cathode properties directly determine the full cell performance and potential commercial development of PIBs.21Table 2 summarizes different full cell configurations and their performance reported so far in the literature.

5.2.1 PB and PBAs for full cell PIBs. 
As discussed above, PB and PBAs are the most commonly used cathode materials in full cell PIBs because of their high voltage platform and excellent cycling stability.78 To improve PB and PBAs’ electrochemical performances, it is usually necessary to control the interstitial water content and formulate PBAs with multiple transition metals. In the past few years, PBAs based on Fe, Mn, and Ni have been reported. The electrochemical performance has been heavily studied in the half-cell configuration.74,79,82 In full cell PIBs, the anode materials used to evaluate the PBA full cell performance are usually carbon-based, such as graphite and AC.461 Glass fiber is commonly selected as the separator between the cathode and anode. Potassium salts, such as KPF6 and KFSA, are dissolved in DEC, PC, EC, DME, or other solvents as the electrolyte.462–465
Lei et al.78 reported K0.220Fe[Fe(CN)6]0.805·4.01H2O nanoparticles (KPBNPs) by a simple co-precipitation method, using commercial carbon black Super P as the anode, and glass fiber (Whatman, Grade GF/B) as the separator. The KPBNP‖carbon Super P full cell was fabricated with 0.8 M KPF6 in EC:DEC = 1:1 vol% as the electrolyte, and the cathode and anode mass ratio was 1:1. Within the full cell charge and discharge in the range of 1.0–3.8 V, the cell exhibited a specific capacity of 71 mA h g at a current density of 0.1 A g−1 and a capacity retention of 93.4% after 50 cycles. Cary L. Pint et al.466 prepared potassium-containing PB (KPB) with a cubic morphology by changing the precipitation conditions, using 1 M KPF6 in diglyme electrolyte to assemble the KPB‖graphite full cell. The cathode and anode mass loading were −0.8 and −0.5 mg cm−2, respectively. At 17.7 °C, the full cell's specific capacity was about 30 mA h g−1, and the capacity retention reached 60% after 2000 cycles at 2 A g−1 (>11C, the specific capacity is about 35 mA h g−1). Shinichi Komaba et al.467 also prepared K1.75Mn[FeII(CN)6]0.93·0.16H2O (K-MnHCFe) via a simple co-precipitation process. Using 0.7 M KPF6 in EC:DEC = 1:1 vol% as the electrolyte to assemble a K-MnHCFe‖graphite full cell, the average working voltage was 3.5 V. The reversible specific capacity was confirmed to be 110 and 60 mA h (g of K-MnHCFe)−1 at a current density of 30 mA g−1 and 2 A g−1, respectively. The energy density was as high as 536 W h kg−1. The same research group further prepared K2Mn[Fe(CN)6],468 which was assembled into a K2Mn[Fe(CN)6]‖graphite full cell with 7 mol kg−1 KFSA in DME as the electrolyte. With a wide voltage range of 0–4.3 V, the full cell provided a specific capacity of 104 mA h (g of K2Mn[Fe(CN)6])−1, and it achieves capacity retention of more than 85% after 101 cycles. Nickel-containing PBAs have also been used for assembling and testing full cell PIBs. Liu et al.469 used K1.81Ni[Fe(CN)6]0.97·0.086H2O (KNHCF) and graphite as the cathode and anode to assemble a KNHCF‖graphite full cell, which showed a capacity of 37.8 mA h g−1 at a working voltage of 3.34 V after 100 cycles (10 mA g−1). The K1.90Ni0.5Fe0.5[Fe(CN)6]0.89·0.42H2O‖graphite full cell reported by Chong et al.470 presented a reversible initial capacity of 84.9 mA h g−1 with an operating voltage of 3.33 V at 10 mA g−1, which could successfully light an LED.
Due to the simple preparation method, high working voltage platform and excellent cycling stability, PBAs such as K2Fe[Fe(CN)6] and K2Mn[Fe(CN)6] prepared by the co-precipitation method are also commonly used as cathodes to evaluate the full cell performance of anode materials, including carbon materials, conversion anodes, and alloy anodes.471,472 Yang et al.218 synthesized 3D carbon nanofiber (CNF) aerogels co-doped with sulfur and nitrogen (S/N-CNFAs) by pyrolyzing seaweed aerogels. A KPB‖S/N-CNFA full cell was assembled using KPB as the cathode and 1 M KPF6 in EC:DEC = 1:1 vol% electrolyte. A specific capacity of 198 mA h (g of S/N-CNFA)−1 was achieved in a voltage range of 2.0–4.2 V (200 mA g−1), which could retain 92% after 60 cycles. Qiu et al.219 designed nitrogen/phosphorus double-doped vertically aligned graphene (N, P-VG@CC) grown on carbon cloth as a flexible non-adhesive anode material and used it to fabricate a full cell with KPB. The cell afforded a high capacity up to 116.25 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1. The cell also achieved a maximum energy density of 232.5 W h kg−1, and the peak power density reaches 4 kW kg−1. The full cell was able to light up 57 green LEDs and power an electronic watch successfully. Wu et al.265 reported a full cell of a highly nitrogen-doped carbon nanofiber (NCNF-650) anode and KPB cathode. At a current density of 0.2 A g−1, the initial specific discharge capacity was 197 mA h (g of NCNF-650)−1.
Regarding conversion-type anodes, our group prepared nano-rose-like MoS2@rGO by a hydrothermal method and fabricated a full cell with a K2Fe[Fe(CN)6] cathode.299 The cell showed an initial discharge specific capacity of 124.9 mA h g−1 at 20 mA g−1, and a capacity retention rate of 95.4% after 10 cycles. Zhan et al.473 used a bottom-up solvothermal method to anchor 1T/2H MoS2 nanosheets on an N-doped carbon nanotube hollow polyhedron, 1T/2H-MoS2/NCNHP. In cooperation with the K2Fe[Fe(CN)6] cathode, the full cell was tested in a voltage window of 1.0–4.0 V. The cell also showed an initial discharge capacity of 136.1 mA h g−1 and a capacity retention rate of 74.2% after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g−1.
Concerning alloy-type anodes, phosphorus-based materials have the highest theoretical specific capacities.434 Tuan et al.435 prepared a composite of red phosphorus with carbon and fabricated a full cell with a KMnHCF cathode. At different charge/discharge rates of 150 and 600 mA g−1, the KMnHCF‖RP/C full cell showed capacities of 450 and 380 mA h g−1 over 40 cycles, respectively. The authors disassembled the cell and found that the full cell's separator has better wettability than in the half-cell. Cao et al.439 prepared SnP3/C nanocomposite anodes by high-energy ball milling and used a KPB cathode to assemble the full cell. The reversible capacity of KPB‖SnP3/C could achieve 305 mA h g−1 in a voltage window of 0.5–3.8 V and after 30 cycles. Chen et al.412 used a 3D macroporous Sb@carbon composite (Sb@C-3DP) as the anode and a KFeHCF cathode. The full cell provided a reversible capacity of 508 mA h (g of Sb@C-3DP)−1 at a current density of 0.2 A g−1 and an energy density of 197.6 W h kg−1. Bi nanorods encapsulated in nitrogen-doped carbon nanotubes (hollow Bi@C nanorods) as the anode and a KPB cathode were used to assemble a full cell by Liu et al.423 The KPB‖Bi@C cell exhibited a specific capacity of 280.5 mA h (g of Bi@C)−1 with a capacity retention of 88.6% after 100 cycles. Bismuth–antimony alloy nanoparticles attached on a porous carbon (BiSb@C) anode and K4Fe(CN)6(KFC) cathode were assembled into a full cell. KFC‖BiSb@C delivered a reversible specific capacity of 396.5 mA h (g of BiSb@C)−1 at a current density of 200 mA g−1.417 He et al.474 assembled a KFeHCF‖Sb0.5Bi0.5@C full cell, which obtained a high reversible capacity of 430 and 225 mA h (g of Sb0.5Bi0.5@C)−1 at 0.05 and 2.0 A g−1, respectively.


5.2.2 Layered transition metal oxides for full cell PIBs. 
Layered transition metal oxide cathodes, e.g. the currently popular high-nickel (NCM), are widely used in full cell LIBs.25 Therefore, similar oxides have also been evaluated as the cathode of PIB full cells.48 Glass fiber is again the most commonly used separator, and the majority of the published studies used KPF6 in EC:DEC (1:1) with different concentrations as the electrolyte.475,476 Ceder et al.108 reported a bulk layered P2 type K0.6CoO2 cathode and assembled the full cell using a graphite anode, which delivered a specific discharge capacity of 53 mA h (g of K0.6CoO2)−1 at a current density of 3 mA g−1. Wang et al.460 prepared P2 type K0.6CoO2 microspheres (s-KCO) and used them to fabricate a full cell with a hard carbon anode. The cell showed a specific charge and discharge capacity of 73 and 72 mA h (g of s-KCO)−1 at 30 mA g−1, respectively. After 50 cycles, the full cell still maintained a reversible specific capacity of 62 mA h g−1. Compared with the above-mentioned bulk morphology of P2-type K0.6CoO2, the microsphere electrode could deliver a better full cell performance, which could be attributed to the suppression of the side reactions between the microspheres and the electrolyte.
Zhang et al.477 reported a P3 type K0.5MnO2 hollow submicrosphere cathode by a self-templating strategy, and completed the full cell with a graphite anode and 0.8 M KPF6 in EC:DEC = 1:1 vol% electrolyte. The full cell showed an energy density of 100.7 W h kg−1 with a cut-off voltage of 3.5 V. To solve the problem of K0.5MnO2 peeling off the electrode at a high cut-off voltage, Zhu et al.478 proposed 2.5 M KFSI in TEP as the electrolyte instead of the traditional EC-based electrolyte, and consequently a cut-off voltage up to 4 V could be employed. The K0.5MnO2‖graphite full cell could provide a reversible capacity of 110.6 mA h (g of K0.5MnO2)−1 and an energy density of 194.6 W h kg−1 at 20 mA g−1. Xu et al.113 achieved some improvements in the full cell performances by increasing the content of K in KxMnO2. The reported K0.77MnO2·0.23H2O‖hard–soft composite carbon full cell exhibited a stable reversible specific capacity of 121 and 70 mA h g−1 at 100 and 1000 mA g−1, respectively.
Binary and ternary layered transition metal oxides have also been evaluated as cathode materials for full cell PIBs. Zheng et al.99 reported the Ni/Mn-based P2 type cathode performance in a full cell using a soft carbon anode. The K0.44Ni0.22Mn0.78O2‖soft carbon cell exhibited initial charge and discharge capacities of 78.9 and 69.2 mA h g−1, respectively. The capacity of the full cell could still maintain 90% at 50 mA g−1 after 500 cycles. Weng et al.479 reported layered K0.7Mn0.7Mg0.3O2 microparticles and assembled a full cell with a hard carbon anode, which showed initial charge and discharge capacities of 86.4 and 73.5 mA h (g of K0.7Mn0.7Mg0.3O2)−1 at 100 mA g−1, respectively. It is worth mentioning here that the full cell performances of ternary P2 type layered transition metal oxide cathodes have also been reported. Sun et al.480 reported the electrochemical evaluation of ternary layered transition metal oxide cathodes in full cells using a hard carbon anode. The K0.75[Mn0.8Ni0.1Fe0.1]O2‖hard carbon full cell showed a capacity retention of 60% after 1000 cycles at 21 mA g−1.


5.2.3 Polyanionic compounds for full cell PIBs. 
Polyanionic compounds have a high working voltage close to that of PBAs.133 In half-cell PIBs, their cut-off voltage can even be set to 5 V, thereby suggesting potential application in high energy density scenarios, which is similar to the commercial use of LiFePO4‖graphite full cells in LIBs.25 In the research on full cell PIBs, these polyanionic compound cathodes are almost limited to vanadate and oxalate.481 Glass fiber is still the commonly used separator. The electrolyte formulation is slightly different, potassium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (KFSI) in EC:EMC (1:1 vol%), but its impact has not been explained. KVPO4F was the first reported batch of high voltage polyanionic compounds suitable for PIB cathodes. Chen et al.358 integrated a KVPO4F-C cathode with the composite Sb2S3@S,N-doped graphene as the full cell's anode. The first specific capacity of the KVPO4F-C‖Sb2S3@S,N-doped graphene cell was 532 mA h (g of Sb2S3-SNG)−1 with an energy density of 166.3 W h kg−1. Chen et al.135 fabricated a KVPO4F‖VPO4 full cell, which showed excellent reversible charge and discharge capacities and a good capacity retention of 86% after 300 cycles at 50 mA g−1. When the current density increased to 100, 200, 500, 1000, 2000, and 5000 mA g−1, the recorded discharge capacities were 102, 101, 99, 97, 92, 84, and 71 mA h g−1, respectively. Yan et al.482 synthesized K0.51V2O5 nanobelts with a large interlayered structure and optimized growth orientation by reconstructing the V–O polyhedra of orthorhombic V2O5. After assembling a K0.51V2O5‖graphite full cell, the reversible specific capacity and energy density of the full cell were 94 mA h (g of K0.51V2O5)−1 and 238 W h kg−1, respectively. A reversible specific capacity of 60 mA h g−1 could also be achieved at a high current density of 2 A g−1, with a capacity retention rate of 84% after 100 cycles.
Tang et al.107 reported iron-based oxalic acid (KLi3Fe(C2O4)3@C) as the cathode for potassium storage. Coupled with a soft carbon anode, the full cell showed reversible specific capacities of 100, 80, 75, and 70 mA h (g of KLi3Fe(C2O4)3@C)−1 at 240, 600, 960 and 1200 mA g−1, respectively. The same authors also reported another high voltage polyanionic compound K4Fe3(C2O4)3(SO4)2 (KFOS),483 which was also tested in a full cell with a soft carbon anode. The capacity retention of KFOS‖soft carbon was above 73% when the current density increased from 200 to 1000 mA g−1. After 200 cycles, the capacity retention was close to 100%. Fluorooxalate (KFeC2O4F) is reported as the cathode for full cell PIBs.143 The full cell, with a soft carbon anode, yielded an energy density of −235 W h kg−1 and could light up more than 40 red and yellow LEDs simultaneously. At current densities increasing stepwise from 0.1 to 0.5 A g−1, the reversible specific capacities of the full cell were 83.7, 71.0, 65.7, 64.7, and 62.6 mA h g−1, respectively. It also maintained excellent cycling stability within 200 cycles at a current density of 0.1 A g−1.


5.2.4 Organic materials for full cell PIBs. 
The unique set of organic materials’ properties (e.g., flexibility and high theoretical specific capacity) makes them strong candidates for developing organic full cell PIBs.163 However, to date, fewer organic materials were reported for assembling full cells, which only include Na2AQ26DS,165 PTCDI,156 PIM,484 PTCDA,485 and AQDS.167 The commonly used separator material is still glass fiber, while the electrolyte for the organic full cell is usually KFSI in DEM.163,485–488
Fan et al.165 reported that polyanionic sodium anthraquinone-2,6-disulfonate (Na2AQ26DS) could be employed as an organic cathode for full cells, and graphite was used as the anode material. The resulting Na2AQ26DS‖graphite full cell can deliver average capacities of −105 mA h (g of Na2AQ26DS)−1 (100 mA g−1) and −64 mA h (g of Na2AQ26DS)−1 for 2500 cycles (500 mA g−1). The same research group reported in another paper perylene-3,4,9,10-tetracarboxylic diimide (PTCDI) as a new organic cathode,156 delivering a stable capacity of −120 mA h g−1 after 600 cycles at 4 A g−1 in half cells. When fabricating into full cells, with a graphite anode, the PTCDI cathode displayed an average capacity of ∼127 mA h g−1 at 50 mA g−1 over 30 cycles. At current densities of 50, 100, 200, 300, and 400 mA g−1, the full cell could hold capacity values of 126, 107, 92, 85, and 80 mA h g−1, respectively. They further developed an insoluble [N,N′-bis(2-anthraquinone)]-perylene-3,4,9,10-tetracarboxydiimide (PTCDI-DAQ) organic cathode.157 Using reduced potassium terephthalate (K4TP) as the organic anode, the full organic cell showed a long stable lifespan (6–2 mA h (g of PTCDI-DAQ)−1 after 10000 cycles), energy density (295 Wh (kg of PTCDI-DAQ)−1), and power density (13800 W (kg of PTCDI-DAQ)−1). Lei et al.484 prepared a PIM@KB organic cathode by depositing polyimide on porous Ketjenblack through a polymerization reaction and assembled a full cell with a nitrogen-doped soft carbon anode. The PIM@KB‖N-soft carbon cell exhibited reversible capacities of 139, 131, 125, 115, and 106 mA h g−1 at different rates of 200, 400, 800, 1600, and 3200 mA g−1, respectively.
The first reported PTCDA as a PIB organic cathode material did not perform well. Lu et al.485 annealed the PTCDA cathode at 450 °C and fabricated it into a full cell with a soft carbon anode in 3M KFSI in DME electrolyte. The PTCDA@450 °C‖soft carbon provided reversible capacities of 116, 109, 94, 80, 70, 63 and 56 mA g−1 at 50, 100, 200, 400, 600, 800 and 1000 mA g−1, respectively. The full cell exhibited long-term stability (capacity retention rate of 65%) at 600 mA g−1 after 3000 cycles. The focus was then switched to red-224 PTCDA as the cathode for full cells, coupled with various anodes. Yan et al.486 selected PTCDA as the cathode to assemble a full cell with an ultrahigh pyridinic N-content-doped porous carbon monolith (PNCM) anode. The full cell could successfully light up an LED watch. Specifically, when the current density increased from 50 to 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA g−1, the specific discharge capacity of the PTCDA‖PNCM full cell only dropped slightly from 245 to 168, 143, 114, 99, and 85 mA h (g of PNCM)−1, respectively. Zhang et al.487 compounded MoS2 with hollow porous carbon spheres (MoS2@HPCS) and used it as the anode in a full cell where PTCDA was the cathode (the voltage window is 0.5–3.5 V). The initial discharge and charge specific capacities of the PTCDA‖MoS2@HPCS full cell were 670.9 and 444.7 mA h g−1, respectively. They also reported a bismuth molybdate microsphere (Bi2MoO6) and evaluated its application potential in a full cell by matching with PTCDA.425 At a current density of 50 mA g−1, the full cell showed a charge and discharge specific capacity of 424.3 and 315.1 mA h g−1, respectively. Zhang et al.488 encapsulated bimetallic selenide (MoSe2/CoSe2) into N/S co-doped hollow carbon nanospheres, which was then connected to rGO (rGO@MCSe). rGO@MCSe was used as the anode to fabricate a full cell with a PTCDA@450 °C cathode, which was used as the anode to fabricate with the PTCDA@450 °C cathode. The full cell showed an initial discharge specific capacity of 114.8 mA h g−1 at 0.1 A g−1 and a capacity retention of 74.1 mA h g−1 after 125 cycles.
PTCDA cathodes have also been used to evaluate the full cell performance of alloy anode materials. Lu et al.489 synthesized (Bi,Sb)2S3 nanotubes by a low-temperature method and used them to assemble a full cell with a PTCDA cathode. The PTCDA‖(Bi,Sb)2S3 full cell presented a reversible specific discharge capacity of 260 mA h (g of (Bi,Sb)2S3)−1 at 500 mA g−1. The initial specific discharge capacity was 276 mA h g−1, which decayed to 222 mA h g−1 after 50 cycles, and then stabilized at 207 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles. In a recent study, bimetallic oxychloride (Bi- and Sb-based oxychloride) nanosheets were grown on rGO (Bi0.51Sb0.49OCl/rGO).427 The full cell with a PTCDA cathode could achieve a high capacity of 285 mA h (g of Bi0.51Sb0.49OCl/rGO)−1 at 100 mA g−1. The full cell PTCDA‖Bi0.5Sb0.5@P was also reported with a reversible specific discharge capacity of 151 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles.490

5.3 Commercialization of full cell PIBs
Although the history of LIBs can be traced back to 1970, production and commercialization of LIBs increased rapidly after 1992.21,25 Intensive research on the commercialization of PIBs is much younger, dating back to 2015. There is only one commercial use of a full cell PIBs by the Chinese company Starsway Electronics to power media player portable devices to the best of our knowledge.
In the above review of PIBs, we found that cathode materials, such as PBAs and PTCDA, are often used for full cell performance evaluation of anode materials, while most of the anode materials still are graphite, which is similar to that of LIBs. Low-cost electrode materials are also favored. However, according to the working mechanism and structure of the full cell, the electrode material is only a small part. In addition, the adaptation of the electrolyte is rarely studied in full cell PIBs. In contrast, the refined electrolyte configuration has been targeted for each type of full cell LIBs. From our point of view, PBA‖graphite (glass fiber, KPF6) would be the first step towards commercialization of full cell PIBs. However, the optimization of electrode materials and electrolytes still needs to be continued.
6. Summary and perspectives
With the exponentially growing consumption of lithium resources in recent years, it is vital to develop alternative technologies for large-scale energy storage to compete with commercialized LIB systems.21 Among the reported rechargeable batteries, PIBs, which are based on using the earth-abundant K element as the charge carrier, are considered suitable for next-generation energy storage systems.45 As graphite is commonly used as an anode for LIBs, it was the first choice anode for PIBs, promoting safety and cycling stability according to some reports.180–183 Moreover, we compared the specific energy, working voltage, cycle life, cost, safety, and the prospects of existing battery systems, such as LIBs, SIBs, Li–S, Na–S, and lead–acid batteries.491–497 As shown in Fig. 16, each kind of battery has different advantages and is thus expected to have various practical application prospects in the future. PIBs exhibit several superiorities, including low cost and high working voltage. However, the major drawbacks are low specific energy, and poor cycle life and safety, which still challenge their potential practical application. The theoretical and practical issues of PIBs are different from LIBs and SIBs because of the larger ionic radius and the higher reactivity of potassium.33 Hence, designing electrode materials and fabricating full cells are quite different in PIBs. One challenge is adjusting the lattice structures of the active materials (such as enlarged interlayer spacing, increased transport channels, low K-intercalation energy barrier, etc.) to facilitate large-radius K+ transport. Other challenges that might appear on a larger scale include the morphological control of the electrode materials to enhance the battery behavior by boosting the kinetics of K+ and electron transport, accommodating the volumetric change, avoiding particle agglomeration and restraining active material dissolution and dispersion. Beyond one particle dimension, the interface interactions (including the electrode–electrolyte interface, single-phase interface of the particles, different-phase interfaces, etc.) are also of great importance to the thermodynamics and kinetics characteristics of the electrode during cycling. To promote PIBs for further application, the design, synthesis, and optimization of electrode materials should be supported by theoretical calculations and in-depth reaction mechanism investigations (Fig. 17). Thus, more research is still needed to meet the requirements of further practical application of high-performance PIBs.
 Fig. 16  Radar plot comparing the specific energy, working voltage, cycle life, cost, safety, and the prospects of PIBs, LIBs, SIBs, Li–S batteries, Na–S batteries, and lead–acid batteries. 
 Fig. 17  The development prospects of PIBs for further practical application. 
6.1 Electrode materials and structures for future technologies

6.1.1 Developing high-performance cathode materials. 
Based on the type of electrochemical reactions, active cathode materials can be categorized into insertion and conversion cathode types. For insertion cathodes, where K+ ions are inserted between the layers of the crystals and then removed in the second half of the cycle, materials such as PB and its analogues, layered transition metal oxides, and flexible organic materials can be used. In this case, a crystal structure that can accommodate the sizeable volumetric change during the K+ ion insertion/deinsertion is crucial for determining the cathode cycle life. Unfortunately, most of the active materials investigated so far cannot sustain substantial volumetric changes, and also exhibited limited storage capacity, and have low conductivity in their native phase. Future research exploring novel flexible structures combined with theoretical prediction would be one of the most effective ways to tolerate the inevitable structural deformation. Also, crystal structure optimization, such as lattice optimization, cationic/anion substitution, and heteroatom doping, could play a dual role in improving the structural stability and optimising the Fermi level to enhance their reversible capacity and long-term cyclability. These methods may also be a potential direction to change the one-electron transfer process of the most promising PB and PB analogue cathodes into multi-electron storage per formula unit with higher capacity and stability. To further improve the electronic conductivity of electrodes, electronic conductive agents (e.g. carbon-based materials) might be a good option to be added into the materials, which could also help to buffer the volumetric changes during cycling. Developing intrinsically conductive cathode materials, probably using potassium-rich phases, or creating more anionic defects, may also help reduce the electrode resistance. Moreover, the reinforcement of PB analogues’ thermal stability and organic cathode materials is also required for further commercial applications. Finally, the essential characteristics and mechanism determination of the reversibility of K+ ion insertion/extraction in insertion-type cathodes should be investigated more deeply to provide references for further developing new high-performance cathode materials.
In conversion cathodes, potassium storage is achieved by solid-state redox reactions with repeated breaking and reforming of chemical bonds during potassiation/depotassiation cycling. These cathode materials, including metal fluorides and chlorides, sulfur, selenium, iodine, and oxygen, always have high theoretical capacity and volumetric capacities. Most of these materials have been used in LIBs and SIBs with better success. Although using them in PIBs is still at an early stage, the results thus far show poor cycling stability due to three competing factors: the poor electrical conductivity, the severe side reactions, and the possible pulverization and/or dissolution of intermediates (e.g. polysulfides). Future studies of these conversion-type cathodes could focus on designing novel low-dimensional nanostructures that can minimize the detrimental effect of the repeated phase changes. Another future direction would be investigating new multi-structure composites that can buffer the contentious changes in the active material, such as carbon yolk–shell structures, or introducing two competing processes, such as mixing two or more active materials. Developing suitable electrolytes and electrolyte additives for the corresponding systems will also improve the cycling stability by minimizing electrode dissolution and parasitic reactions. Using potassium-rich materials might also control the formation of intermediate phases, hence improving the cathode's stability. Indeed, optimizing the cathode production method is necessary to reduce costs and achieve a high energy density before practical application.


6.1.2 Designing stable nano-structural anode materials. 
The research on the anode materials is still dominated by carbon-based materials, with some attempts to use alloy-based materials, layered metal compounds, metal–organic framework materials, and organic materials. The selection of these materials is mainly based on their reported success with LIBs, with few attempts to design anode materials specifically for potassium systems. Despite the clear efforts, there are still several challenges in developing suitable carbon-based electrode materials with reasonable potassium-storage capability. Some metal-based compound anodes, such as titanium-based compounds, have shown high reversible capacities, and good cycling stability, but their low energy density still limits their practical applications. Anodes based on materials that can alloy with potassium exhibited high specific capacities and energy density. However, they present finite cycling stability due to the serious volume and phase variations with further particle pulverization and aggregation. The dwindling contact among particles fades their capacity rapidly. Intercalation-based anodes will probably be ruled out as candidates for PIBs shortly since inserting the large size of K+ in the defined interlayer space will make any improvement in their performance incremental. To facilitate the commercial feasibility of PIBs, future research should focus on improving the storage capacity and long-term stability based on conventional anode materials. Nano-architectural design and engineering of more robust anode structures should be investigated more with a focus on improving the cycling and rate performance. The new designs should tolerate sizeable volume variation, resist pulverization, and increase the electrode/electrolyte interface area. Engineering low dimensional materials into porous 3D architectures may help in buffering the volumetric changes and shorten the diffusion pathways. More active sites could be exposed to the electrolyte, which facilitates the K+ reaction with the anode with increasing the specific surface area. Multi-structure design and morphology optimization on heterostructure composite electrodes, including all kinds of hierarchical structures, hollow structures, coated structures, sandwich-type structures, yolk–shell structures etc., could become promising strategies to alleviate the mechanical stress during charge/discharge cycling. The formed internal electric field at the interfaces of the coupled materials could bring rapid diffusion while improving the electronic/ionic conductivity and the electrochemical activity. Functionalizing the anode materials to have more target groups on the surface is another way to enhance the K+ kinetics by reducing the adsorption energy. Of course, the preparation process of the designed nano-architectural materials should be low cost, simple to control, and easy to automatic fabrication for further practical application.

6.2 Theoretical calculations for PIBs

6.2.1 DFT calculations supporting the improvement of the electrode performance. 
Since the development of entirely new active materials is difficult, optimizing the current materials’ composition seems more practical. Heteroatom doping of anode materials, typically with N, S, O, P, F, and/or B, has shown great success with LIB and SIB anodes but has not been studied enough in PIBs. The defects or vacancies forming in the heteroatom doping sites’ vicinity force electrons to rearrange, altering the electronic structure or the hybridization states. This electron redistribution has a significant influence on improving the reaction kinetics and the electrode electrical conductivity. Doping carbonaceous materials with nitrogen, especially pyridinic N, is known to enhance the K–C adsorption bond around the nitrogen doping sites. Some reports have linked nitrogen doping to improved initial reversible capacity in all alkali-ion batteries. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations suggest that doping carbon materials with P, S, F, and B atoms improves the electron transfer and enlarges the interlayer distance, leading to stabilizing the structures and improving the theoretical capacities. However, no experimental data have been published yet. The so-called co-doping (doping using more than one element) can improve the electrode performance by all the dopants’ synergistic effect. For example, co-doping hard carbon materials with N and O could simultaneously enhance the capacity and cycling performance. DFT calculations and further experimental research on various combinations of multi-element doped electrodes, such as N/S doped, N/P doped, S/O doped, P/B doped, and N/P/S doped carbon, should be investigated carefully in the near future.
Similarly, replacing the cations in the cathode material lattice with a different valence cation or inducing defects in the anionic parts may also enhance the cathode's conductivity and reactivity toward K+. Similar strategies have been successfully used with LIB and SIB cathodes, but very few experimental or theoretical studies can be found for PIBs. Material simulation techniques could help optimize the cathode materials by determining the type and level of doped cations. It could also help predict the properties of the materials when a certain level of defects is introduced in the anion sites. Co-doping cathode materials with more than one cation or with cations and anions should also be investigated theoretically in the future to benefit from the synergy between the dopants.


6.2.2 In-depth understanding of the electrochemical reaction mechanism. 
More theoretical and experimental studies are needed to gain more insights into the electrochemical reaction mechanism and the changes in both electrodes during charge/discharge. During the potassiation process on both electrodes, several intermediate phases are formed. The properties of theses phases are not the same as the starting materials. Material simulation might help determine the properties of the intermediate phases, such as their conductivity and solubility in the electrolyte, increasing the electrode stability. Of particular interest, the mechanism of forming the SEI layer should receive more attention. Engineering a robust SEI through ALD, CVD, or similar techniques would undoubtedly help prevent unwanted reactions on the electrode/electrolyte interface, preventing severe electrolyte decomposition and improving the electrode's overall stability. In the near future, advanced in situ spectroscopic characterization techniques (e.g. cryogenic electron microscopy, HRTEM, STEM, Raman spectroscopy, and Fourier transform infrared microscopy) with supporting DFT calculations could help to understand the K ion transport through the SEI, as well as get more details on the side reactions and SEI formation process. More focus should be on understanding the reactions in the electrolyte. DFT calculations can bring more insights on the relation between anions and cations in the electrolytes and the interaction between ions and the base solvent. The transport mechanism and kinetics of K ions in different electrolytes should also receive more attention in the future.

6.3 Advanced in situ characterization techniques for PIBs
During the charging and discharging process of alkali metal-ion batteries such as LIBs, SIBs and PIBs, ion migration and movement cause changes in the cathode, anode, and electrolyte. The electrochemical performance of the cell is closely related to the internal structure or composition changes during cycling. Many analytical techniques have been used to study the battery structure and chemical changes, most of which are ex situ. These ex situ techniques cannot obtain accurate information, particularly about instantaneous processes that might produce unstable phases. On the other hand, in situ techniques are useful to collect real-time data about the chemical reactions or phase changes during electrochemical potassiation/depotassiation.

In situ characterization has been widely used to study LIBs, including in situ XRD, Raman, SEM, TEM, etc. However, the mechanisms of electrochemical reactions of K+ with most electrode materials are not well understood as in the LIB case. Therefore, more applications of in situ techniques in PIB research are anticipated in the future. In situ TEM and XRD should be carried out to collect more real-time data that might help understand the K+ ion insertion/extraction process and SEI film formation in PIB systems. It is perhaps important from the experimental perspective to design new cell systems that would allow real-time monitoring of the electrochemical processes. For example, performing XPS or FTIR on the electrolyte/electrode interface would enable more knowledge of the surface reactions and the formation of the SEI film. However, these kinds of studies will require a careful design of the specific experimental rig. In the future, the accuracy and applicability of the existing in situ analytical technique should be improved, perhaps also designing new instruments exclusively for PIBs. Furthermore, it will be great to see some development in combining more than one in situ technique. Collecting more than one type of data simultaneously will be useful for a better understanding of several spontaneous and fast processes.
6.4 Optimizing full cell design for applications
Most of the research carried out thus far is focused on the active materials, with the majority of the lab tests conducted on half-cell configurations using potassium metal as the counter electrode. In this case, it is known that the exact cycling behavior of the working electrode might be concealed by the side reactions between the potassium metal electrode and electrolytes. For instance, the Coulombic efficiency of carbon-based anodes (e.g. hard carbon, CNTs, and graphene) is always less than 70%, which, of course, severely limits their practical application. In order to move PIBs closer to commercial use, other areas, such as electrolytes, electrolyte additives, and binders, should also be investigated. More attention should also be paid to optimizing full cells using the best-known electrolyte and electrode materials.

6.4.1 Electrolyte matching and optimization. 
The electrolytes, as the window for electrochemical reactions by connecting the electrodes, are significantly crucial for the development of commercial PIBs. On the interface of the electrolytes and electrodes, a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer passivation film is usually formed on the electrode surface because of the irreversible reduction of electrolytes. In PIBs, the SEI layer is thick, implying poor ionic diffusion kinetics and high resistance. One route to improve the ionic conductivity of the SEI layer is by optimizing the electrolyte (alkali salts and solvents) and investigating new additives to manage the SEI formation. Suppressing dendrite growth and minimizing the side reactions also need to be considered in the new electrolyte system development process. Since the amount of electrolyte in the actual full cell is limited, issues related to dendrite formation, the SEI layer, and side reactions would significantly influence the cell's overall performance. Reports have shown that the KFSI electrolyte has higher conductivity than KPF6, but issues related to the composition's homogeneity should be addressed. Electrolytes that use ether-based solvents (e.g. DMC, DME, and diglyme) are reported to form more stable SEI layers with higher initial Coulombic efficiency than carbonate-based electrolytes (e.g. EC, DEC, and PC) due to their strong chemical adsorption and charge-transfer kinetics. Some binary or ternary solvent mixtures (e.g. KFSI–DME electrolyte), polymer electrolytes, and potassium ionic liquids could also promote the formation of stable SEI layers and suppress dendrite growth. In addition, the SEI layers formed with some electrolyte additives, such as FEC, could also prevent the side reactions and stay more stable to a certain extent.


6.4.2 Binders and separators. 
The electrode fabrication technology is a problem shared by all the alkali-metal ion batteries. One of the issues related explicitly to PIBs is the type of binder. The polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder, commonly used in LIBs, cannot sustain the large volumetric changes associated with the potassium ion insertion/stripping. As a result, exfoliating the key functional components away from the metal foil current collector is very common in PIBs, leading to fast capacity fading and poor electronic conductivity. Therefore, more studies focusing on exploring new binders with excellent elasticity are necessary to improve the electrodes’ mechanical integrity for practical PIBs. Further development of separators is also desirable to optimize their performances. Modifying the separators’ sides that face the anode or the cathode could be a viable approach to suppress the parasitic reactions and dendrite formation.

6.5 Battery safety
Although PIBs have shown lower susceptibility to thermal runaway and in general release less heat than LIBs, the safety of PIBs remains a problem due to the flammable organic electrolytes and low thermal stability SEI layers. Using thermally stable separators, polymer electrolytes, and solid-state electrolytes, and adding flame-retardants into the liquid electrolytes or electrode materials may improve battery safety. Theoretical and experimental studies should also be utilized to build an efficient kinetic model of the complex thermal runaway process, facilitating the unique structural design of high-safety PIBs.
In summary, PIB systems based on the abundant potassium resource have presented remarkable potential for large-scale energy storage. Although there are, to date, still enormous challenges before PIBs achieve commercial viability, it is believed that all these problems could be addressed in future studies. Therefore, outlining the present technologies and proposing possible perspectives of new batteries will help us better understand rechargeable PIB systems and promote faster progress towards their real applications in the future.

Xin Min
	Assoc. Prof. Xin Min received his BS degree (2011) and PhD degree (2016) from School of Materials Science and Technology, China University of Geosciences (Beijing). Then, he joined the faculty of School of Materials Science and Technology, China University of Geosciences (Beijing). He is a visiting scholar in Department of Materials and Metallurgy, University of Cambridge. His main research interests include micro-/nanostructured functional materials and nanocomposites in energy storage and conversion.

Jun Xiao
	Jun Xiao received his Bachelor's degree in School of Materials Science and Technology, China University of Geosciences (Beijing) in 2019. He is currently pursuing his Master's degree under the supervision of Assoc. Prof. Xin Min and Prof. Minghao Fang. His research focuses on the electrode materials of potassium ion batteries.

Minghao Fang
	Prof. Minghao Fang received his BS degree (2000) and PhD degree (2005) from School of Materials Science and engineering, Tsinghua University. He joined the faculty of School of Materials Science and Technology, China University of Geosciences (Beijing), in 2005. He engaged in the main research on advanced composite ceramics and one-dimensional ceramic fibers.

Wei (Alex) Wang
	Wei (Alex) Wang is a professor in School of Space and Environment at Beihang University (BUAA). He received his PhD degree in State Key Laboratory of Advanced Metallurgy in 2016 from University of Science and Technology Beijing and studied at University of Cambridge as a joint PhD student. Before joining BUAA in 2019, he was a postdoctoral researcher at Peking University and Harvard University. His research focuses on renewable energy conversion and storage technology. He has authored/co-authored over 100 research papers and filed 15 patents.

Kai Xi
	Kai Xi is a research associate in the Cambridge Graphene Centre. He obtained his PhD degree in the Department of Materials Science and Metallurgy, University of Cambridge. His research focusses on materials engineering and physical chemistry for renewable energy conversion and storage, in particular lithium-sulphur (Li-S) batteries, lithium/sodium/potassium ion batteries and solar cells. He was awarded the grand prize of the Dow Sustainability Innovation Student Challenge Award. His CamBattery team been awarded Technology Start Up of the Year by Cambridge University Entrepreneurs in 2012.

Broader context
The current limited and uneven distributions of lithium resources in the world, coupled with the rapid growth of electric vehicles and the dependence on renewable energy, have motivated the pursuit for efficient alternatives to commercial lithium-ion batteries (LIBs). Rechargeable potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) are promising candidates due to the abundance of potassium resources, high operating voltage, and considerable theoretical capacity. Nevertheless, this battery system faces serious challenges for the requirements of commercialization, such as short cycle life, low rate performance, and limited practical capacities. To get closer toward the industrial utilization of PIBs, all the battery components should be carefully optimized and thoroughly studied. Here, the latest developments on PIBs are systematically reviewed, and innovative electrode designs are highlighted to inspire future research and development. The challenges and limitations of each electrode material are summarized alongside the reaction mechanisms and the electrode/electrolyte interaction. This review introduces new insights and an outlook, and suggests future directions and challenges, which can be used as a platform for further developing and optimizing PIBs, moving them forward to wider and large-scale applications.
